Objections: Support Specificity and Timing of Restriction
Author presents two objections: support specificity and restriction timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key logical objections to the Shia reading: that support directed to a group cannot include themselves, and that restriction (hasr) only applies when there is doubt, which did not exist regarding the first three caliphs.

Core Idea
The Shia interpretation fails on logical and historical grounds.

After establishing the linguistic meaning, the author presents 'other faces of objection'. The first objection argues that if the verse is addressing a specific group, the support must refer to others outside that group. The second objection points out that the concept of restriction only applies in contexts of dispute, and at the time of revelation no one doubted the imamate of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. Let's see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the Arabic and translation for this section. Note the structure: 'minhā anna...' (among them is that).
2
Understand the first objection: support cannot include the group itself
If support (nusrah) is directed to a specific group of believers, then logically that group cannot be supporting themselves. So the verse must be referring to other believers outside that group. Imam al-Razi praises this as a precise argument.
Consider how this undermines the Shia claim that the verse points to Ali and his followers.
3
Understand the second objection: restriction presupposes prior doubt
The term 'hasr' (restriction) is used only when there was hesitation or dispute. At the time of revelation, there was no dispute about the imamate of the first three caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman). Therefore, the verse cannot be restricting imamate to Ali.
This objection addresses the historical context of the revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى : ومن يتولهم منكم فإنه منهم ) (1) لظهور أن ذلك تولي محبة ونصرة لا إمامة . وبالجملة لا يخفى على من تأمل في سياق الآية وكان له معرفة بأساليب الكلام أن ليس المراد بالولي فيها ما يقتضي الإمامة ، بل الموالاة والنصرة والمحبة ، ثم وصف المؤمنين لما ذكر يجوز أن يكون للمدح والتعظيم دون التقييد والتخصيص، وأن يكون لزيادة شرف الموصوفين واستحقاقهم أن يتخذوا أولياء وأولويتهم بذلك، وقربهم ونصرتهم، وشفقتهم الحاملة على النصرة وقوله : وهم راكعون كما يحتمل الحال يحتمل العطف، بمعنى أنهم يركعون في صلاتهم لا كصلاة اليهود خالية عن الركوع أو بمعنى أنهم خاضعون . على أن ههنا وجوهاً أخر من الاعتراض، منها أن النصرة وإن كانت عامة، لكن إذا أضيفت إلى جماعة مخصوصة من المؤمنين فبالضرورة تختص بمن عداهم، لأن الإنسان لا يكون ناصراً لنفسه. وكأنه قيل لبعض المؤمنين : إنما ناصركم البعض الآخر. قال الإمام الرازي : إن هذا السؤال عليه التعويل في دفع هذه الشبهة (٢) فإنه دقيق متين، وأنت خبير بأن مبناه على اختصاص الخطاب بالبعض من المؤمنين، وعلى كون المؤمنين الموصوفين جميع من عداهم. ومنها أن الحصر إنما يكون نفياً لما وقع فيه تردد ونزاع (۳) ولا خفاء في أن ذلك عند نزول الآية لم يكن إمامة الأئمة الثلاثة (٤) . ومنها أن ظاهر الآية ثبوت الولاية بالفعل، وفي الحال، ولا شبهة في أن إمامة علي (رضي الله عنه) إنما كانت بعد النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) والقول بأنه كانت له ولاية التصرف في أمر المسلمين في حياة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) أيضا مكابرة. وصرف الولاية إلى ما يكون في المآل دون الحال لا يستقيم في حق الله تعالى ورسوله . ومنها أن الذين آمنوا صيغة جمع ، فلا يصرف إلى الواحد إلا بدليل . وقول المفسرين إن الآية نزلت في حق علي رضي الله عنه لا يقتضي اختصاصها به واقتصارها عليه ودعوى انحصار الأوصاف فيه مبنية على جعل (وهم راكعون) حالا من ضمير يؤتون وليس بلازم . ومنها أنه لو كانت في الآية دلالة على إمامة علي (رضي الله عنه) لما خفيت على الصحابة عامة . وعلى علي خاصة، ولما تركوا الانقياد لها والاحتجاج بها .
And His statement exalted: 'And whoever takes them as allies among you, then indeed he is of them' (1) because it is apparent that that is taking as allies in love and support, not imamate. And in summary, it is not hidden from one who ponders the context of the verse and has knowledge of the styles of speech that the intended meaning of 'wali' in it is not what implies imamate, but rather alliance, support, and love. Then the description of the believers for what was mentioned is possible to be for praise and glorification without restriction and specification, and to be for increasing the honor of the described ones and their deservingness that they take allies and their priority in that, and their closeness and their support, and their compassion that impels to support. And His statement: 'while they bow' can be a circumstantial accusative (ḥāl) or a conjunctive (ʿaṭf), meaning that they bow in their prayer, not like the prayer of the Jews devoid of bowing, or meaning that they are humble. Moreover, there are other faces of objection here: among them that support, although general, when it is added to a specific group of believers, it necessarily becomes specific to others besides them, because a person is not a supporter of himself. And it is as if it was said to some believers: 'Your supporter is only the other group.' Imam al-Razi said: 'This question is relied upon in repelling this doubt (2), for it is precise and solid, and you are aware that its basis is on the specification of the address to some of the believers, and on the believers described being all those other than them.' And among them (the objections) that restriction (ḥaṣr) is only a negation of what occurred in it hesitation and dispute (3), and it is no secret that at the time of the revelation of the verse that was not the imamate of the three imams (4). And among them that the apparent meaning of the verse is the establishment of guardianship (wilāya) actually and in the present, and there is no doubt that the imamate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) was only after the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the claim that he had authority to act in the affairs of the Muslims during the Prophet's life is also stubbornness. And turning the guardianship to what will be in the future rather than the present is not valid with respect to Allah and His Messenger. And among them that 'those who believe' is a plural form, so it is not restricted to one except with evidence. And the statement of the commentators that the verse was revealed concerning Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) does not require its exclusivity to him and restriction upon him. And the claim of the exclusivity of the attributes to him is based on making ('while they bow') a circumstantial accusative of the pronoun of 'they give' and it is not necessary. And among them that if the verse had indication of the imamate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), it would not have been hidden from the Companions in general, nor from Ali in particular, and they would not have abandoned compliance with it and argumentation by it.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shia claim that Quran 5:51 supports the imamate of Ali. The author first argues that the context shows 'wali' here means love and support, not imamate. He then discusses the phrase 'while they bow' (raaki'un), offering two possible interpretations: as a circumstantial accusative indicating they bow in prayer, or as an adjective meaning they are humble. Following this, he enumerates several objections (wujuh al-i'tirad) against the Shia reading. First, support when directed to a specific group necessarily excludes the group itself. Imam al-Razi endorses this point. Second, the concept of restriction (hasr) is used only where doubt exists, and at revelation there was no doubt about the imamate of the first three caliphs. Third, the verse speaks of actual present guardianship, whereas Ali's imamate came later. Fourth, 'those who believe' is plural and cannot be restricted to one person without evidence; the claim of exclusive attributes to Ali is based on a non-necessary grammatical interpretation. Fifth, if the verse indicated Ali's imamate, it would not have been hidden from the Companions. Thus the author systematically dismantles the Shia interpretation through both linguistic analysis and logical argument.

Key Terms
نصرة (support)
حصر (restriction)
إمامة (imamate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The restriction in the verse automatically applies to the imamate of Ali.
Correction: Restriction is only meaningful where there is prior dispute, which did not exist regarding the imamate of the first three caliphs.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Imam al-Razi's praise for the first objection?
A) It shows the verse is metaphorical
B) It relies on the specificity of the address and the inclusiveness of the described believers
C) It proves that Ali was the only supporter
D) It uses grammatical ambiguity

Key Takeaway
The Shia reading fails because support cannot be self-directed and the verse's restriction does not apply to imamate due to historical context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a proof for a belief because it felt satisfying, even though a closer look at the evidence would undermine it? What would it take for you to honestly re-examine that belief using the kind of rigorous reasoning shown in this passage?
?
When you hear a verse of the Quran used to support a particular position, do you always check when it was revealed and what the historical context was? If not, what is stopping you from doing that, and how might that neglect allow falsehood to take root in your heart?
?
This lesson shows two precise objections that dismantle a false interpretation. Do you have the intellectual humility to accept that you might be wrong about something you deeply believe? If your whole community taught you something, would you have the courage to question it if the evidence pointed the other way?

Objections: Timing, Plurality, and Companion Consensus
Author presents three objections: present vs future, plural form, and companion consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three further objections: that the verse speaks of present guardianship not future imamate, that the plural form cannot be restricted to one person, and that if it indicated imamate it would not have been hidden from the Companions.

Core Idea
The verse's language and historical context disprove the Shia claim.

The author continues with more objections. The third objection points out that the verse speaks of current wilaya, whereas Ali's imamate came later. The fourth objection notes that 'those who believe' is plural, so cannot be restricted to one. The fifth objection argues that if the verse indicated imamate, the Companions would have known and acted on it.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Read the Arabic and translation for this section. Notice the structure: 'minhā anna...' repeated three times.
2
Understand the timing objection
The verse speaks of wilaya in the present tense ('thubūt al-wilāya bil-fiʿl, fī al-ḥāl'). Ali's imamate was only after the Prophet's death. Claiming he had authority during the Prophet's life is stubbornness.
This shows a mismatch between the verse and history.
3
Understand the plurality objection
The phrase 'those who believe' (alladhīna āmanū) is plural. It cannot be restricted to one person without clear evidence. The claim that it refers exclusively to Ali is based on a non-necessary grammatical reading of 'while they bow' as a circumstantial accusative.
This objection targets the Shia reliance on one grammatical possibility.
4
Understand the consensus objection
If the verse clearly indicated Ali's imamate, it would not have been hidden from the Companions in general, nor from Ali himself. They would have used it as proof.
This is an argument from historical silence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shia claim that Quran 5:51 supports the imamate of Ali. The author first argues that the context shows 'wali' here means love and support, not imamate. Fifth, if the verse indicated Ali's imamate, it would not have been hidden from the Companions. The Shia Argument from Hadith Ghadir and Manzila
The author presents the Shia argument from Ghadir and Manzila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shia use the hadiths of Ghadir and Manzila to claim Ali's exclusive right to imamate.

Core Idea
The Shia interpret 'mawla' as 'ruler' and 'manzila' as all authority except prophethood.

This lesson presents the third Shia argument for imamate, based on two well-known hadiths. The author first states the Shia interpretation before refuting it. Understanding their claims is essential to grasping the Sunni counter-arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia argument passage
Read the passage presenting the Shia interpretation of the Ghadir and Manzila hadiths. Note how they derive the claim of Ali's imamate.
The author is not endorsing this view; he states it to refute it.
2
Understand the interpretation of 'mawla'
The Shia argue that 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith means 'the one in authority'. They support this with Quranic and hadith usage, like 'Fire is your mawla' and 'marriage guardian'.
3
Understand the interpretation of 'manzila'
The Shia interpret 'You are to me as Harun was to Musa' as granting Ali all of Harun's stations except prophethood, thus including political succession.
This is a key logical step: 'Manzila' is general with the definite article.
4
See how these lead to imamate
From these interpretations, the Shia conclude that Ali was designated as the rightful successor (caliph/imam) after the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الثالث - (ما تواتر) من حديث الغدير والمنزلة، فإن المراد بالمولى المتولي للأمر ، والأولى بالتصرف فيه، كما في قوله تعالى : (مأواكم النار هي مولاكم) (٢) . وقوله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن مولاها (٣) لا المعتق والمعتق، والحليف والجار، وابن العم وهو ظاهر، ولا الناصر فإنه ظاهر، ومنزلة هارون من موسى (عليهما السلام) عام بمنزلة المعرف باللام . فحيث أخرجت النبوة، تعينت الخلافة والتصرف في أمر العامة لو بقي بعده. وهي معنى الإمامة. والجواب منع التواتر، بل الكلام في صحة خبر الغدير ودلالته على حصر الإمامة في علي (رضي الله عنه)، ثم لا عبرة بالآحاد في مقابلة الإجماع ، وترك عظماء الصحابة الاحتجاج بهما آية عدم الدلالة والحمل على العناد غاية الغواية، ولو سلم عموم المنزلة بالإضافة إلى العلم ، فلا يتناول الخلافة، والتصرف بطريق النيابة
He said: The third - what has been transmitted widely from the hadith of Ghadir and (the hadith of) the Station, for the intended meaning of 'mawla' is the one in charge of the affair and the one most entitled to dispose of it, as in His statement exalted: 'Your abode is the Fire; it is your mawla' (2). And His statement (peace be upon him): 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her mawla (guardian)' (3) — not the freed slave and the manumitter, nor the ally and the neighbor, nor the cousin — and this is apparent; nor the supporter, for it is apparent. And the station (manzila) of Harun relative to Musa (peace be upon them) is general as a station determined by the definite article. So where prophethood is excluded, the caliphate (khilafa) and disposal of the affairs of the general public are determined if he had remained after him. And that is the meaning of imamate. The answer: denying the widespread transmission (tawatur); rather the discussion is about the authenticity of the hadith of Ghadir and its indication of restricting imamate to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Then isolated reports (ahad) have no weight against consensus (ijma'). And the abandonment of the great Companions to argue by them is evidence of lack of indication, and attributing it to obstinacy is extreme error. And if the generality of the station is conceded with respect to knowledge, it does not include the caliphate and disposal by way of deputyship.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the third Shia argument based on two hadiths: the hadith of Ghadir (where the Prophet said 'For whomever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla') and the hadith of Manzila ('You are to me as Harun was to Musa, except that there is no prophet after me'). The author first explains the Shia interpretation: the word 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith means the one in authority, supported by Quranic and hadith usage (e.g., 'Fire is your mawla' and 'marriage guardian'). The hadith of Manzila is understood to grant Ali all of Harun's stations except prophethood, implying succession after the Prophet. Thus the Shia deduce Ali's imamate. The author then provides a refutation: first, he denies that these hadiths are mutawatir (massively transmitted), questioning their authenticity. Second, even if they are authentic, they are ahad (isolated reports) and cannot override the consensus (ijma') of the Companions. Third, the fact that the great Companions did not use these hadiths to support Ali's imamate is strong evidence that they lack the claimed indication. Fourth, even if one accepts the generality of the 'station' (manzila), it pertains only to knowledge, not to political authority and governance. Thus the author refutes the Shia position on multiple grounds.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla)
الغدير (Ghadir)
المنزلة (Manzila)
خلافة (khilafa)
إمامة (imamah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the hadith of Ghadir explicitly states Ali's imamate.
Correction: The Shia interpret it that way, but the author will show the interpretation is not explicit and the hadith is not mutawatir.

Checkpoint
According to the Shia interpretation presented in the passage, what does the word 'mawla' in the hadith of Ghadir mean?
A) A freed slave
B) A friend
C) The one in authority
D) A neighbor

Key Takeaway
The Shia argue that the hadiths of Ghadir and Manzila establish Ali's imamate by interpreting 'mawla' as ruler and 'manzila' as general authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith of Ghadir, does your prior commitment to a particular school of thought color how you interpret the word 'mawla'? Would you be willing to accept the plain linguistic evidence if it contradicted your tradition?
?
If you had been a Companion at Ghadir Khumm, hearing the Prophet's words, what would have prevented you from understanding 'mawla' as authority? Is it intellectual honesty or inherited tradition that shapes your understanding?
?
The Shia build their entire political theology on two hadiths. How carefully do you examine the textual evidence for your own core beliefs about Islamic leadership? Are you willing to revise if the evidence points elsewhere?

Sunni Refutation of the Shia Argument
The author refutes the Shia argument with four key objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four-fold Sunni refutation of the Shia argument from the hadiths of Ghadir and Manzila.

Core Idea
The hadiths are not mutawatir, their interpretation is weak, and the Companions' silence disproves them.

Having presented the Shia claims, the author now responds with a robust refutation. He argues that the hadiths are not mass-transmitted (mutawatir), that isolated reports (ahad) cannot override consensus (ijma'), that the Companions' failure to use these hadiths as proof shows their lack of indication, and that even if accepted, the 'station' only covers knowledge, not political authority. This lesson breaks down each point.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the author's response carefully. Notice he begins by denying the widespread transmission (tawatur) of the hadiths.
2
Identify the first refutation: Weak Transmission
The author denies that the hadiths are mutawatir, meaning they are not reliably mass-transmitted. He implies they are ahad (isolated), which are insufficient for essential doctrine.
This is a key methodological point in Islamic theology.
3
Identify the second refutation: Ahad vs Ijma'
Even if the hadiths were authentic, they are ahad reports and cannot override the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, who did not interpret them as the Shia do.
4
Identify the third refutation: Lack of use by Companions
The fact that the great Companions (like Abu Bakr, Umar) did not use these hadiths to support Ali's claim is evidence that they do not indicate his exclusive imamate.
If the hadiths were clear, the Companions would have used them.
5
Identify the fourth refutation: Limited scope of Manzila
Even conceding the generality of 'manzila', it only extends to knowledge, not to political authority (khilafa) and governance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الثالث - (ما تواتر) من حديث الغدير والمنزلة، فإن المراد بالمولى المتولي للأمر ، والأولى بالتصرف فيه، كما في قوله تعالى : (مأواكم النار هي مولاكم) (٢) . وقوله (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن مولاها (٣) لا المعتق والمعتق، والحليف والجار، وابن العم وهو ظاهر، ولا الناصر فإنه ظاهر، ومنزلة هارون من موسى (عليهما السلام) عام بمنزلة المعرف باللام . فحيث أخرجت النبوة، تعينت الخلافة والتصرف في أمر العامة لو بقي بعده. وهي معنى الإمامة. والجواب منع التواتر، بل الكلام في صحة خبر الغدير ودلالته على حصر الإمامة في علي (رضي الله عنه)، ثم لا عبرة بالآحاد في مقابلة الإجماع ، وترك عظماء الصحابة الاحتجاج بهما آية عدم الدلالة والحمل على العناد غاية الغواية، ولو سلم عموم المنزلة بالإضافة إلى العلم ، فلا يتناول الخلافة، والتصرف بطريق النيابة
He said: The third - what has been transmitted widely from the hadith of Ghadir and (the hadith of) the Station, for the intended meaning of 'mawla' is the one in charge of the affair and the one most entitled to dispose of it, as in His statement exalted: 'Your abode is the Fire; it is your mawla' (2). And His statement (peace be upon him): 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her mawla (guardian)' (3) — not the freed slave and the manumitter, nor the ally and the neighbor, nor the cousin — and this is apparent; nor the supporter, for it is apparent. And the station (manzila) of Harun relative to Musa (peace be upon them) is general as a station determined by the definite article. So where prophethood is excluded, the caliphate (khilafa) and disposal of the affairs of the general public are determined if he had remained after him. And that is the meaning of imamate. The answer: denying the widespread transmission (tawatur); rather the discussion is about the authenticity of the hadith of Ghadir and its indication of restricting imamate to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Then isolated reports (ahad) have no weight against consensus (ijma'). And the abandonment of the great Companions to argue by them is evidence of lack of indication, and attributing it to obstinacy is extreme error. And if the generality of the station is conceded with respect to knowledge, it does not include the caliphate and disposal by way of deputyship.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the third Shia argument based on two hadiths: the hadith of Ghadir (where the Prophet said 'For whomever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla') and the hadith of Manzila ('You are to me as Harun was to Musa, except that there is no prophet after me'). The author first explains the Shia interpretation: the word 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith means the one in authority, supported by Quranic and hadith usage (e.g., 'Fire is your mawla' and 'marriage guardian'). The hadith of Manzila is understood to grant Ali all of Harun's stations except prophethood, implying succession after the Prophet. Thus the Shia deduce Ali's imamate. The author then provides a refutation: first, he denies that these hadiths are mutawatir (massively transmitted), questioning their authenticity. Second, even if they are authentic, they are ahad (isolated reports) and cannot override the consensus (ijma') of the Companions. Third, the fact that the great Companions did not use these hadiths to support Ali's imamate is strong evidence that they lack the claimed indication. Fourth, even if one accepts the generality of the 'station' (manzila), it pertains only to knowledge, not to political authority and governance. Thus the author refutes the Shia position on multiple grounds.

آحاد (ahad)
إجماع (ijma')
دلالة (dalala)
عناد (inad)

Correction: The author shows they are not mutawatir and their interpretation is contested by the silence of Companions.

B) The hadiths are unsound in chain

Key Takeaway
The Sunni refutation argues the hadiths are not mutawatir, their interpretation is not exclusive, and the Companions' consensus and silence undermine the Shia claim.

?

?

?
If you truly believe that isolated reports cannot override consensus, will you change how you evaluate controversial rulings that are based on weak or solitary hadith? What is one specific issue where you need to surrender your personal preference to the weight of scholarly consensus?

Refuting the Shia Proof from Verse 5:55
The author refutes the Shia claim from verse 5:55 using linguistic and historical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic verse 5:55 does not prove Ali's exclusive imamate, based on linguistic and historical arguments.

Core Idea
A plural verse cannot prove a single person's exclusive role without clear evidence.

This passage from Sanusi's work refutes a key Shia prooftext for Ali's imamate: verse 5:55. Sanusi offers two arguments: one from grammar (the plural form cannot specify Ali alone) and one from history (if the verse were proof, the Companions would have known). Before reading, note that the author is engaging in inter-sectarian debate, using rational and traditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noticing the two main arguments. Don't worry about details yet; just get the flow.
The passage is dense, so read slowly.
2
Analyze the first argument
Focus on the linguistic point: the plural 'those who believe' cannot refer to a single person without evidence. Commentators' statements that it was revealed about Ali do not make it exclusive. Also note the grammatical point about 'while they bow'.
This argument targets the claim that the verse is about Ali alone.
3
Analyze the second argument
Consider the historical argument: if the verse indicated Ali's imamate, the Companions and Ali himself would have known and used it as evidence. They did not, so the claim fails.
This appeals to the consensus of the early community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الذين آمنوا صيغة جمع ، فلا يصرف الى الواحد إلا بدليل . وقول المفسرين إن الآية نزلت في حق علي رضي الله عنه لا يقتضي اختصاصها به واقتصارها عليه ودعوى انحصار الأوصاف فيه مبنية على جعل وهم راكعون ) (۱) حالا من ضمير يؤتون وليس بلازم . ومنها أنه لو كانت في الآية دلالة على إمامة علي رضي الله عنه) . لما خفيت على الصحابة عامة . وعلى علي خاصة، ولما تركوا الانقياد لها والاحتجاج بها .
And among them is that 'those who believe' is a plural form, so it is not turned to the singular except by evidence. And the statement of the commentators that the verse was revealed concerning Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) does not necessitate its specification to him and its restriction to him. And the claim of the restriction of the attributes to him is based on making 'and they are bowing' a state from the pronoun of 'they give', and it is not necessary. And among them is that if there were in the verse an indication of the imamate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), it would not have been hidden from the Companions in general, and from Ali in particular, and they would not have abandoned submission to it and using it as evidence.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage refutes the Shia argument that verse 5:55 ('Indeed your wali is only Allah, and His Messenger, and those who believe, who establish prayer and give zakah while they bow') indicates Ali's imamate. The author gives two main points: (1) The phrase 'those who believe' is plural and cannot refer to a single person without a clear indicator. (2) Even if the verse was revealed about Ali, as some commentators say, that does not mean it exclusively refers to him; the plural form remains general. (3) The claim that the attributes mentioned (faith, prayer, zakah, bowing) are exclusive to Ali relies on interpreting 'while they bow' as a circumstantial clause describing 'they give', which is not grammatically necessary. (4) If the verse truly indicated Ali's imamate, it would not have been hidden from the Companions, including Ali himself, and they would not have failed to use it as evidence in the matter of succession.

Key Terms
الآية
صيغة جمع
الإمامة
الصحابة
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that a verse revealed about a specific person exclusively applies to that person in all its rulings.
Correction: The author clarifies that the plural form of the verse keeps it general unless strong evidence restricts it, and revelation occasion does not automatically specify.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against interpreting 'those who believe' in verse 5:55 as referring exclusively to Ali?
A) The verse was revealed about all believers
B) The phrase is plural and cannot be restricted to one without evidence
C) The verse was abrogated
D) Ali denied it

Key Takeaway
The plural wording of verse 5:55 and the historical silence of the Companions collectively undermine the claim that it proves Ali's exclusive imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran, do you focus more on the story of its revelation than on its actual wording? How does ignoring the plural form in 5:55 change your understanding of who is being addressed? Will you start paying more attention to the linguistic structure of verses?
?
The author argues that if the verse proved Ali's imamate, the Companions—who knew the verse and loved Ali—would not have ignored it. Is there any belief you hold about early Islamic history that you have never doubted because it seemed obvious? What would it take for you to subject it to the same scrutiny?
?
The refutation shows that even a verse revealed about a specific person does not automatically prove a unique, exclusive role for them. Are there any practices or loyalties in your life that you assume are divinely sanctioned based on a single incident or story, without considering the broader evidence from the Quran and Sunnah? Will you re-examine them honestly?

The Shia Argument from the Hadith of Ghadir and Manzilah
The author presents the Shia claim from the mutawatir hadiths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Shia scholars use the hadith of Ghadir and Manzilah to argue for Ali's authority and imamate.

Core Idea
Two mutawatir hadiths are used to claim Ali's authority over the community.

This passage presents a key Shia argument relying on two well-known hadiths. The first, the Hadith of Ghadir, interprets the word 'mawla' to mean 'master' or 'one with authority.' The second, the Hadith of Manzilah, draws an analogy between Ali and Harun, excluding only prophethood. Together, they are used to establish Ali's right to leadership after the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the argument present itself before you interpret.
The passage uses very concise scholarly language.
2
Identify the two hadiths and their interpretations
Note how the author defines 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith and how the Manzilah hadith is applied. Notice the specific evidence cited.
The Quranic verse (57:15) and the prophetic tradition about marriage permission are used to support the definition of mawla.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Understand the final inference: since prophethood is excluded, only caliphate and general authority remain. This is the core of the Shia claim for imamate.
The definite article in 'al-manzila' is emphasized to indicate completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الثالث - ما تواتر من حديث الغدير والمنزلة، فإن المراد بالمولى المتولي للأمر ، والأولى بالتصرف فيه، كما في قوله تعالى : مأواكم النار هي مولاكم ) (۲) . وقوله ( ) : أيما امرأة نكحت بغير إذن مولاها (۳) لا المعتق والمعتق، والحليف والجار، وابن العم وهو ظاهر، ولا الناصر فإنه ظاهر، ومنزلة هارون من موسى (عليهما السلام) عام بمنزلة المعرف باللام . فحيث أخرجت النبوة، تعينت الخلافة والتصرف في أمر العامة لو بقي بعده. وهي معنى الإمامة.
He said: The third – that which is mutawatir from the hadith of Ghadir and Manzilah, for the intended meaning of al-mawla is the one in charge of the affair, and the one with priority in disposal in it, as in His statement: 'Your abode is the Fire; it is your mawla' (57:15), and his saying ( ) : 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her mawla' – not the freed slave or the one who freed, the ally, the neighbor, and the cousin – that is apparent, nor the helper – that is apparent. And the station of Harun from Musa (peace be upon them) is general, like the station defined by the definite article. So when prophethood is excluded, the caliphate and disposal in the affairs of the general public is determined if he had remained after him. And that is the meaning of imamate.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is the presentation of the Shia's third argument, claiming two mutawatir hadiths: the hadith of Ghadir and the hadith of Manzilah. It argues that the word 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith means the one who has authority and priority in disposal, citing Quranic and prophetic usage. Then it argues that the hadith of Manzilah (where the Prophet said to Ali, 'You are to me as Harun was to Musa, except that there is no prophet after me') establishes that Ali has a general station like Harun's, which includes authority over the community. Since prophethood is excluded, the only remaining station is that of caliphate (khilafa) and general authority, which is the meaning of imamate. The use of the definite article in 'al-manzila' (the station) indicates completeness and generality.

Key Terms
التواتر
حديث الغدير
حديث المنزلة
المولى
الخلافة
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Manzilah hadith only indicates love and friendship between Ali and the Prophet, not authority over the community.
Correction: The Shia argument interprets 'al-manzila' with the definite article to mean a general station that includes all of Harun's roles except prophethood, which necessarily includes leadership and authority over the community.

Checkpoint
According to the Shia argument presented, what does 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith mean?
A) Helper
B) Freed slave
C) The one in charge and priority in disposal
D) Cousin

Key Takeaway
The Shia third argument for imamate relies on the mutawatir hadiths of Ghadir and Manzilah to establish Ali's authority analogous to the Prophet's authority, excluding only prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet explicitly designated Ali at Ghadir, what is the real reason you have not followed him? Is it because of honest doubt, or because acknowledging it would uproot your entire religious identity?
?
The station of Harun includes leading the people while Moses was alive. If you deny that same role for Ali, what does that say about your trust in the Prophet's words? Are you willing to follow the truth even if it means parting from your community?
?
Who is your true mawla today? If it is not the one designated by the Prophet, on what basis do you choose? Is it evidence, tradition, or convenience?

Refuting the Shia Argument from Authenticity and Consensus
Sunni teacher refutes Shia using transmission and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni response to the Shia claim that the Hadith of Ghadir proves exclusive imamate for Ali, focusing on the arguments from tawatur and the priority of consensus over solitary reports.

Core Idea
A solitary hadith cannot override the consensus of the Companions.

The author begins his refutation by challenging the claim that the Hadith of Ghadir is mutawatir. He then argues that even if authentic, it is a solitary report (ahad) that cannot be used against the consensus (ijma) of the Companions who elected Abu Bakr. The fact that the senior Companions did not rely on this hadith further shows it does not indicate imamate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shia claim
The Shia argue that the Hadith of Ghadir is mutawatir and clearly designates Ali as the immediate successor.
This is the claim the author refutes.
2
Examine the first refutation: not mutawatir
The author states the 'jawab' (answer) begins by rejecting tawatur (mass transmission). The hadith is not mutawatir in the view of Sunni scholars.
3
Study the second refutation: ahad vs ijma
Even if the hadith is authentic, it is a solitary report (ahad). In Islamic legal theory, ahad reports cannot overturn the consensus (ijma) of the Companions, which established the caliphate of Abu Bakr.
This is a key methodological point.
4
Consider the Companions' silence
The fact that the great Companions did not use this hadith as evidence at the Saqifah or elsewhere shows that it does not actually indicate imamate. Accusing them of obstinacy is extreme deviation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع التواتر، بل الكلام في صحة خبر الغدير ودلالته على حصر الإمامة في علي (رضي الله عنه ، ثم لا عبرة بالآحاد في مقابلة الإجماع ، وترك عظماء الصحابة الاحتجاج بهما آية عدم الدلالة والحمل على العناد غاية الغواية، ولو سلم عموم المنزلة بالإضافة إلى العلم ، فلا يتناول الخلافة، والتصرف بطريق النيابة لأنه شريك في النبوة، ولا يدل على بقائها، بعد موت المستخلف، وليس انتفاؤها عزلاً ونقصاً ، بل عوداً إلى الكمال، وهو الاستقلال. وتصرف هارون لو بقي إنما [؟] يكون لنبوته . وقد انتفت في حق علي رضي الله عنه) فكذا ما يبني عليها
And the answer is to reject tawatur; rather the discussion is about the authenticity of the hadith of Ghadir and its indication of restricting the imamate to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Then there is no reliance on isolated reports (ahad) in the face of consensus (ijma'). And the fact that the great Companions left out using them as evidence is a sign of lack of indication, and attributing it to obstinacy is extreme deviation. Even if the generality of the Manzilah is granted with respect to knowledge, it does not include the caliphate and disposal by way of deputyship because he would be a partner in prophethood, and it does not indicate its continuance after the death of the one who appointed him (the Prophet). And its removal is not an expulsion or deficiency, but rather a return to perfection, which is independence. And the disposal of Harun, had he remained, would only be due to his prophethood. And that has been negated in the case of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). So likewise what is built upon it.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is the Sunni response to the Shia's third argument. The author first denies that the hadiths are mutawatir (mass-transmitted). Then he argues that even if the hadith of Ghadir is authentic, it does not prove exclusive imamate for Ali. He gives multiple reasons: (1) The hadith is a solitary report (ahad) and cannot override the consensus (ijma') of the Companions on the caliphate of Abu Bakr. (2) The senior Companions did not use this hadith as evidence when they decided on the caliphate, which shows it does not actually indicate imamate. To accuse them of obstinacy is extreme. (3) Even if the Manzilah hadith is accepted as general, it does not entail khilafa because Harun's authority came from his prophethood. Ali was not a prophet, so the analogy fails. Moreover, Harun's deputyship was during Musa's lifetime; it did not continue after Musa's death. Similarly, Ali's station was during the Prophet's life, not after. The removal of that station (when the Prophet died) is not an expulsion but a return to independence, which is perfection. Therefore, the Shia claim collapses.

Key Terms
التواتر
الآحاد
الإجماع
الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Ghadir is definitively proven (mutawatir) and clearly indicates Ali's immediate succession.
Correction: The Sunni response denies its tawatur and argues that even if authentic, it is a solitary report that cannot overrule the consensus of the Companions, and the Companions' silence shows it does not indicate imamate.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the Hadith of Ghadir cannot be used to prove Ali's exclusive imamate?
A) The hadith is fabricated
B) It is not mutawatir and is a solitary report that cannot override ijma
C) The Companions agreed it was abrogated
D) The hadith only refers to a military command

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response denies the tawatur of the Hadith of Ghadir and asserts that solitary reports cannot annul the established consensus of the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain of something, are you willing to submit to the consensus of trustworthy people if they disagree with your personal evidence? What would that cost you?
?
Have you ever clung to a proof or argument because it felt strong to you, even though those with deeper knowledge set it aside? Are you ready to humbly re-examine your convictions today?
?
If you were in the position of the Companions and had heard the Hadith of Ghadir, would you have the courage to remain silent if you knew it did not mean what some claimed? How do you handle ambiguity in matters of truth?

Refuting the Manzilah Hadith: Scope and Harun Analogy
Sunni refutation of Manzilah hadith argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars refute the Shia use of the Hadith of Manzilah, by showing it only pertains to knowledge during the Prophet’s lifetime and that Harun’s deputyship was due to his prophethood, which Ali lacked.

Core Idea
Ali’s station was like Harun’s in knowledge, not in prophetic authority or succession.

The author now addresses the Manzilah hadith ('You are to me as Harun was to Musa'). Even if the general meaning is accepted, it does not entail caliphate or deputyship after the Prophet's death. Harun's authority came from his prophethood, which Ali did not have. The removal of the deputyship after the Prophet's death was not a demotion but a return to independence, which is perfection.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Shia interpretation of Manzilah
The Shia argue that just as Harun was Musa's deputy, so Ali was the Prophet's deputy, implying succession to authority.
2
See the Sunni limitation: applies to knowledge only
The author says even if we grant general similarity, it is limited to knowledge ('ilm), not to authority or caliphate.
3
Understand the Harun analogy: authority from prophethood
Harun's authority (tasarruf) came because he was a prophet. Ali was not a prophet, so the analogy for deputyship fails.
4
Grasp the temporary nature of deputyship
Harun was Musa's deputy during Musa's life. After Moses died, Harun's deputyship ended. Similarly, Ali's station ended with the Prophet's death, and returning to independence is a perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع التواتر، بل الكلام في صحة خبر الغدير ودلالته على حصر الإمامة في علي (رضي الله عنه ، ثم لا عبرة بالآحاد في مقابلة الإجماع ، وترك عظماء الصحابة الاحتجاج بهما آية عدم الدلالة والحمل على العناد غاية الغواية، ولو سلم عموم المنزلة بالإضافة إلى العلم ، فلا يتناول الخلافة، والتصرف بطريق النيابة لأنه شريك في النبوة، ولا يدل على بقائها، بعد موت المستخلف، وليس انتفاؤها عزلاً ونقصاً ، بل عوداً إلى الكمال، وهو الاستقلال. وتصرف هارون لو بقي إنما [؟] يكون لنبوته . وقد انتفت في حق علي رضي الله عنه) فكذا ما يبني عليها
And the answer is to reject tawatur; rather the discussion is about the authenticity of the hadith of Ghadir and its indication of restricting the imamate to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). Then there is no reliance on isolated reports (ahad) in the face of consensus (ijma'). And the fact that the great Companions left out using them as evidence is a sign of lack of indication, and attributing it to obstinacy is extreme deviation. Even if the generality of the Manzilah is granted with respect to knowledge, it does not include the caliphate and disposal by way of deputyship because he would be a partner in prophethood, and it does not indicate its continuance after the death of the one who appointed him (the Prophet). And its removal is not an expulsion or deficiency, but rather a return to perfection, which is independence. And the disposal of Harun, had he remained, would only be due to his prophethood. And that has been negated in the case of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). So likewise what is built upon it.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is the Sunni response to the Shia's third argument. The author first denies that the hadiths are mutawatir (mass-transmitted). Then he argues that even if the hadith of Ghadir is authentic, it does not prove exclusive imamate for Ali. He gives multiple reasons: (1) The hadith is a solitary report (ahad) and cannot override the consensus (ijma') of the Companions on the caliphate of Abu Bakr. (2) The senior Companions did not use this hadith as evidence when they decided on the caliphate, which shows it does not actually indicate imamate. To accuse them of obstinacy is extreme. (3) Even if the Manzilah hadith is accepted as general, it does not entail khilafa because Harun's authority came from his prophethood. Ali was not a prophet, so the analogy fails. Moreover, Harun's deputyship was during Musa's lifetime; it did not continue after Musa's death. Similarly, Ali's station was during the Prophet's life, not after. The removal of that station (when the Prophet died) is not an expulsion but a return to independence, which is perfection. Therefore, the Shia claim collapses.

Key Terms
المنزلة
الخلافة
النبوة
الاستقلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Manzilah hadith proves that Ali was the Prophet's deputy with full authority to rule after his death.
Correction: The Sunni response shows it only applies to knowledge during the Prophet's life, and the Harun analogy actually undermines the Shia claim because Harun's authority came from prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the Manzilah hadith not prove Ali's authority as a deputy after the Prophet's death?
A) The hadith is weak
B) Harun's authority was due to his prophethood, which Ali lacks
C) The Companions rejected it
D) The hadith is about military matters

Key Takeaway
The Manzilah hadith only indicates a similarity in knowledge, not authority, and the Harun analogy actually disproves Shia claims because Harun's authority was prophetic and temporary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold someone in high regard for their knowledge, do you automatically assume they should have authority in other areas of your life? How does this lesson's sharp distinction between knowledge and authority challenge a tendency in your own heart? Will you examine your assumptions today?
?
The author describes the removal of Ali's deputyship as a 'return to perfection' (istiqlal). Is there a role, position, or responsibility you are clinging to that you believe defines you, but which letting go would actually be a 'return to perfection' for you? What would that look like, and what is stopping you?
?
Are you quick to embrace analogies or arguments that seem to support your preferred position, without scrutinizing their limits? This lesson shows how the Harun analogy actually works against the Shia claim. Can you recall a time you used an analogy that seemed to support you but later realized it had a flaw? What did you learn from that, and how can you guard against that tendency in the future?

The Event of Ghadir Khumm
Narration of the Ghadir event and the hadith text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical event of Ghadir Khumm, the text of the Prophet's declaration about Ali, and the hadith's acceptance among the companions.

Core Idea
An authentic hadith where the Prophet declared Ali as mawla, implying his high status.

The Ghadir hadith is a cornerstone of Shia arguments for succession. In this passage, the author narrates the event, including the hot day, the gathering, and the Prophet's words. We will examine this narration and the claim that it was undisputed during the Shura.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith narration
Read the Arabic passage describing the day of Ghadir and the Prophet's words. Notice the setting and the question 'Alastu awla bikum min anfusikum?'
The weather detail emphasizes the importance.
2
Identify the key phrase
The Prophet says 'man kuntu mawlahu fa'aliyyun mawlahu'. Consider what 'mawla' might mean based on this context.
3
Note the claim of acceptance
The author states that Ali mentioned this hadith during the Shura and that no one denied it. This is a claim about its widespread acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك بما يدعون فيه التواتر من الأخبار، أما حديث الغدير فهو أنه (عليه السلام) قد جمع الناس يوم غدير خم موضع بين مكة والمدينة - بالجحفة وذلك بعد رجوعه من حجة الوداع. وكان يوماً صائفاً حتى إن الرجل ليضع رداءه تحت شدة الحر، وجمع الرحال، وصعد (عليه السلام) عليها وقال مخاطباً معاشر المسلمين : ألست أولى بكم من أنفسكم؟ قالوا: اللهم بلى. قال: فمن كنت مولاه فعلى مولاه اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه، وانصر من نصره واخذل من خذله (۱) وهذا حديث متفق على صحته أورده علي رضي الله عنه يوم الشورى عندما حاول ذكر فضائله، ولم ينكره أحد . ولفظ المولى قد يراد به المعتق، والمعتق، والحليف، والجار ، و ابن العم والناصر والأولى بالتصرف، قال الله تعالى: (مأواكم النار هي مولاكم ) (٢) أي أولى بكم ذكره أبو عبيدة وقال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن مولاها (٣) . . أي الأولى بها والمالك لتدبير أمرها، ومثله في الشعر كثير. وبالجملة استعمال المولى بمعنى المتولي والمالك للأمر، والأولى بالتصرف شائع في كلام العرب منقول عن كثير من أئمة اللغة والمراد أنه اسم لهذا المعنى، لا صفة، بمنزلة الأولى ليعترض بأنه ليس من صيغة اسم التفضيل ، وأنه لما يستعمل استعماله، وينبغي أن يكون المراد به في الحديث هو هذا المعنى ليطابق صدر
They cling to what they claim is mutawatir of the reports. As for the hadith of Ghadir: it is that he (peace be upon him) gathered the people on the day of Ghadir Khumm, a place between Makkah and Madinah, at al-Juhfah, after his return from the Farewell Pilgrimage. It was a hot day such that a man would place his cloak under his feet due to the intense heat. He gathered the saddles, ascended them, and addressed the Muslims: 'Am I not more worthy of you than yourselves?' They said: 'O Allah, yes.' He said: 'So for whomever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla. O Allah, befriend those who befriend him, and oppose those who oppose him, and help those who help him, and abandon those who abandon him.' This is a hadith agreed upon for authenticity, mentioned by Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) on the day of the Shura when he tried to mention his virtues, and no one denied it. The word 'mawla' can be used for the freed slave, the one who freed, the ally, the neighbor, the cousin, the helper, and the one with priority in disposal. Allah said: 'Your abode is the Fire; it is your mawla' (57:15), meaning more worthy of you, mentioned by Abu 'Ubayda. And the Prophet (s) said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her mawla,' i.e., the one with priority over her and the owner of her affairs, and similar usage is abundant in poetry. In summary, the use of mawla in the sense of the one in charge and owner of the affair, and the one with priority in disposal, is common in Arabic speech, transmitted from many imams of language. And the meaning is that it is a noun for this meaning, not an adjective, like 'first' so that it is not objected that it is not a superlative form, and that it is used in that way. And it should be that the intended meaning in the hadith is this meaning to match the beginning [of the hadith].
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the Shia's reliance on the Ghadir hadith and then provides a detailed linguistic analysis of the word 'mawla'. The author narrates the hadith, emphasizing that it was mentioned by Ali during the Shura and not denied, implying its authenticity. Then he lists the various meanings of 'mawla' in Arabic: freed slave, manumitter, ally, neighbor, cousin, helper, and the one with priority in disposal. He cites Quranic and prophetic usage to show that 'mawla' can mean 'awla' (more worthy/prior). He quotes Abu 'Ubayda's interpretation of the Quranic verse and the hadith about marriage without permission. He concludes that the common usage among Arabs is that 'mawla' means the one in charge and the owner of the affair, i.e., the one with priority in disposal. It is a noun, not an adjective, so it is not subject to the rules of comparative forms. Therefore, the intended meaning in the Ghadir hadith should be this meaning of priority in disposal to match the Prophet's opening question, 'Am I not more worthy (awla) of you than yourselves?' However, the author is implying that this does not necessarily mean succession; it could simply be a command to love and support Ali. The truncated ending likely continues the argument.

Key Terms
حديث الغدير
غدير خم
الشورى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim the hadith is unreliable or fabricated.
Correction: The author asserts it is mutawatir (widely transmitted) and agreed upon for authenticity, and that Ali used it without denial.

Checkpoint
Where did the Ghadir event take place?
A) In Makkah during the pilgrimage
B) In Madinah after the pilgrimage
C) At Ghadir Khumm between Makkah and Madinah
D) At the hill of Arafat

Key Takeaway
The Ghadir hadith recounts the Prophet's declaration about Ali in a specific setting, and it was cited by Ali without objection during the Shura.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet gathered people in extreme heat to deliver a message. What does this tell you about the importance he placed on clarifying relationships of authority? Are you willing to endure discomfort to align your loyalties with what the Prophet commanded?
?
The Prophet prayed: 'O Allah, befriend those who befriend him and oppose those who oppose him.' Examine your own heart: is there anyone from the Prophet's family—especially Ali—whom you have neglected, opposed, or failed to support? What specific step can you take today to be among those befriended?
?
On the Day of Shura, Ali used this hadith as evidence, and no one denied it. If you were a Companion present that day, would you have remained silent? What keeps you from testifying to the truth even when it is uncomfortable or inconvenient?

The Linguistic Meaning of Mawla
Linguistic analysis of the word 'mawla' and its application to the Ghadir hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple meanings of the Arabic word 'mawla' and how the author argues that in the Ghadir hadith it means 'the one with priority in disposal'.

Core Idea
The word 'mawla' has many meanings, but the Ghadir context points to 'priority in disposal'.

The crux of the debate over Ghadir revolves around the word 'mawla'. The author provides a linguistic analysis, listing its possible meanings and citing Quranic and prophetic usage. He then argues that the intended meaning should be 'al-awla bi al-tasarruf' (the one with priority in disposal) to match the beginning of the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic analysis
Read the list of meanings for 'mawla' and the examples from the Quran and Hadith. Notice how each meaning is illustrated.
2
Understand the semantic range
The word can mean freed slave, ally, neighbor, cousin, helper, etc. But the author highlights that it is commonly used for the one in charge.
The example from the Quran: 'ma'wakumu an-nar hiya mawlakum' meaning more worthy.
3
Connect to the hadith
The author concludes that in the Ghadir hadith, 'mawla' should mean the one with priority in disposal to align with the Prophet's initial question about being 'awla' (more worthy).

Refuting the Aaron Analogy for Imamate
The author refutes the analogy between Aaron's deputyship and Ali's claim to Imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy of Aaron's deputyship does not support Ali's claim to Imamate, because Aaron's authority was based on prophethood which Ali did not possess.

Core Idea
Aaron's authority came from prophethood, not deputyship; thus the analogy fails.

In debates over the Imamate, some have argued that just as Aaron was deputized by Moses, Ali should have been the immediate successor. But this analogy overlooks a crucial difference: Aaron's authority was rooted in his prophethood, not merely in being a deputy. In this lesson, we will examine the author's precise refutation of this comparison.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full argument in the author's own words.
Pay attention to the key term 'شريك في النبوة' (partner in prophethood).
2
Identify the core refutation
Notice that the author first states Aaron was a partner in prophethood, then argues that the cessation of his deputyship was not a demotion but a return to independence. Understand this line of reasoning.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that since Ali lacked prophethood, anything built on that analogy is invalid. Reflect on why this is a decisive refutation.
4
Reflect on common misunderstandings
Consider the common misconception that the analogy is strong because Aaron was a deputy. The commentary helps clarify why this is wrong.
This guards against superficial reading of historical analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه شريك في النبوة، ولا يدل على بقائها، بعد موت المستخلف، وليس انتفاؤها عزلاً ونقصاً ، بل عوداً إلى الكمال، وهو الاستقلال. وتصرف هارون لو بقي إنما يكون لنبوته . وقد انتفت في حق علي رضي الله عنه) فكذا ما يبني عليها
Because he (Aaron) was a partner in prophethood, and it does not indicate its continuation after the death of the one who appointed him, and its removal is not a dismissal or deficiency, but a return to perfection, which is independence. And the authority of Aaron if he remained would only be due to his prophethood. And it is negated in the case of Ali (may God be pleased with him), so likewise what is built upon it.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Shi'a argument that uses the analogy of Aaron's deputyship to argue for Ali's Imamate. The author first states that Aaron was a partner in prophethood, meaning his authority derived from his prophetic status, not merely from being a deputy. The succession of Moses by Aaron was temporary and did not imply that such succession would continue after Moses's death. In fact, the cessation of Aaron's authority upon Moses's return was not a demotion but a return to the proper state of independence. The author then argues that if Aaron had remained, his actions would have been due to his prophethood, not simply deputyship. Since Ali did not have prophethood, the analogy fails. Therefore, any argument built on this analogy is invalid.

Key Terms
شريك في النبوة
الاستقلال
التصرف
نبوة
إمامة
عزل
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Shi'a analogy is strong because Aaron was a deputy of Moses, showing that succession by appointment is valid.
Correction: The analogy fails because Aaron's authority was based on his prophethood, not simply on deputyship. Ali did not have prophethood, so the analogy does not apply.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for rejecting the analogy of Aaron as a proof for Ali's Imamate?
A) Aaron was not actually a deputy of Moses.
B) Aaron's authority came from his prophethood, not deputyship.
C) Aaron died before Moses, so the analogy is incomplete.
D) The analogy is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Aaron analogy fails because Aaron's authority derived from prophethood, which Ali did not possess; therefore any argument for Imamate based on this analogy is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on flawed analogies to justify your own desires or actions? How can you ensure that your reasoning in matters of faith is sound, especially when the evidence is clear?
?
The passage argues that Aaron's authority was prophetic, not merely deputyship. Are there areas in your life where you claim authority or responsibility without the proper qualifications? What does this teach you about humility and accepting your limitations?
?
When you encounter arguments that seem strong but are fundamentally flawed, do you have the courage to question and refute them, even if they come from respected sources? What might be holding you back from seeking the truth wholeheartedly?

The Hadith of Ghadir and its Authenticity
The text narrates the Hadith of Ghadir and asserts its mass transmission and acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the narration of the Hadith of Ghadir and the Shi'a claim of its mass transmission and acceptance.

Core Idea
The Hadith of Ghadir is presented as a universally accepted proof for Ali's Imamate.

The Shi'a rely on the Hadith of Ghadir as a central proof for the Imamate of Ali. This lesson presents the narration as recorded in the text, focusing on its context and the claim of its widespread acceptance. You will examine the hadith's content and the assertion that it was agreed upon as authentic.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith narration
Read the Arabic passage describing the event at Ghadir Khumm. Notice the setting: the return from Hajj, the hot day, the gathering.
2
Identify the key question and declaration
The Prophet asks: 'Am I not more worthy of you than yourselves?' and then declares: 'For whomever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla.' Understand the connection.
3
Note the claim of authenticity
The text states the hadith is 'agreed upon as authentic' and that Ali cited it during the Consultation without denial. Consider the significance of this claim.
This claim is contested by Sunnis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك بما يدعون فيه التواتر من الأخبار، أما حديث الغدير فهو أنه (عليه السلام) قد غدير خم موضع بين مكة والمدينة - بالجحفة وذلك بعد رجوعه من حجة الوداع. وكان يوماً صائفاً حتى إن الرجل ليضع رداءه تحت شدة الحر، وجمع الرحال، وصعد (عليه السلام) عليها وقال مخاطباً معاشر المسلمين : ألست أولى بكم من أنفسكم؟ قالوا: اللهم بلى. قال: فمن كنت مولاه فعلی ،مولاه اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه، وانصر من نصره خذله (۱) واخذل من وهذا حديث متفق على صحته أورده علي رضي الله عنه يوم الشورى عندما حاول ذكر فضائله، ولم ينكره أحد . ولفظ المولى قد يراد به المعتق، والمعتق، والحليف، والجار ، و ابن العم والناصر والأولى بالتصرف، قال الله تعالى: (مأواكم النار هي مولاكم ) (٢) أي أولى بكم ذكره أبو عبيدة وقال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن مولاها (٣) . . أي الأولى بها والمالك لتدبير أمرها، ومثله في الشعر كثير. وبالجملة استعمال المولى بمعنى المتولي والمالك للأمر، والأولى بالتصرف شائع في كلام العرب منقول عن كثير من أئمة اللغة والمراد أنه اسم لهذا المعنى، لا صفة، بمنزلة الأولى ليعترض بأنه ليس من صيغة اسم التفضيل ، وأنه لما يستعمل استعماله، وينبغي أن يكون المراد به في الحديث هو هذا المعنى ليطابق صدر الحديث، ولأنه لا وجه للخمسة الأول وهو ظاهر ولا للسادس لظهوره وعدم ، احتياجه إلى البيان وجمع الناس لأجله، سيما وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿ والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ) (1) . ولا خفاء في أن الولاية بالناس والتولي والمالكية لتدبير أمرهم ، والتصرف فيهم بمنزلة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) هو معنى الإمامة .
They hold fast to what they claim to be mass-transmitted (tawatur) among the reports. As for the Hadith of Ghadir, it is that he (peace be upon him) was at Ghadir Khumm, a place between Mecca and Medina at al-Juhfa, after his return from the Farewell Pilgrimage. It was a hot day such that a man would place his cloak under his feet due to the intensity of the heat. He gathered the mounts and ascended upon them and said, addressing the community of Muslims: 'Am I not more worthy (awla) of you than yourselves?' They said: 'O Allah, yes.' He said: 'For whomever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla. O Allah, befriend him who befriends him, and be hostile to him who is hostile to him, and help him who helps him, and abandon him who abandons him.' And this is a hadith agreed upon as authentic; Ali (may God be pleased with him) cited it on the day of the Consultation (al-Shura) when he tried to mention his virtues, and no one denied it. The word 'mawla' may refer to the freed slave, the patron, the ally, the neighbor, the cousin, the helper, and the one more entitled to act (al-awla bi al-tasarruf). God the Most High said: 'Your refuge is the Fire; it is your mawla' i.e., more worthy of you, as Abu Ubayda mentioned. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Any woman who marries herself without the permission of her mawla' i.e., the one more worthy of her and the owner of her affair (al-malik li-tadbir amriha). Similar usage is abundant in poetry. In summary, the use of 'mawla' in the sense of the one in charge (al-mutawalli), the owner of the affair (al-malik li al-amr), and the one more entitled to act is widespread in the speech of the Arabs, transmitted from many imams of language. It is intended that it is a noun for this meaning, not an adjective, like 'awla', so that one does not object that it is not of the superlative form, and that it is used like it. And it should be that the intended meaning in the hadith is this meaning, to match the beginning of the hadith, and because there is no justification for the first five (meanings), which is obvious, nor for the sixth due to its obviousness and its lack of need for explanation and the gathering of people for it, especially since God the Most High said: 'And the believing men and believing women are allies (awliya) of one another.' There is no doubt that authority over the people, taking charge, and ownership of managing their affairs, and acting in them like the Prophet (peace be upon him), is the meaning of Imamate.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the Shi'a argument for Imamate based on the Hadith of Ghadir. It first narrates the hadith: at Ghadir Khumm, the Prophet asked if he was more worthy (awla) of the believers than themselves; they agreed. Then he said, 'For whomever I am his mawla, Ali is his mawla. O Allah, befriend him who befriends him, and be hostile to him who is hostile to him...' The text claims this hadith is agreed upon as authentic. Then it discusses the meaning of 'mawla'. It lists seven possible meanings: freed slave, patron, ally, neighbor, cousin, helper, and the one more entitled to act. It provides Quranic and prophetic evidence that 'mawla' can mean 'the one more worthy' (awla). The author then argues that the intended meaning in the hadith is 'the one in charge of the affairs' (al-mutawalli, al-malik li al-amr), which is exactly the meaning of Imamate. The reasoning is that the other meanings are not plausible in the context, especially given the large gathering and the need for explanation. Thus, the Shi'a claim that this hadith designates Ali as the Imam.

Key Terms
حديث الغدير
التواتر
الولاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, where did the Hadith of Ghadir take place?
A) In Mecca
B) At Ghadir Khumm
C) In Medina
D) On the way to Tabuk

Key Takeaway
The Hadith of Ghadir is presented as a mass-transmitted, agreed-upon hadith in which the Prophet designates Ali as mawla, understood by Shi'a as the first in authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate a hadith like Ghadir, are you willing to examine your own biases towards the narrators or the sect that preserved it? What would it take for you to accept or reject a narration that challenges your current beliefs?
?
The Prophet asked 'Am I not more worthy (awla) of you than yourselves?' and they affirmed. How do you personally respond to that question today—do you truly place his authority above your own desires and opinions? Where do you resist?
?
If the Hadith of Ghadir were presented to you as an irrefutable proof, would you dare to question the interpretation of 'mawla' even if it meant opposing those you trust? Are you prepared to defend your understanding before Allah?

The Meaning of 'Mawla' and the Argument for Imamate
The text analyzes the word 'mawla' and argues it means 'the one in charge' in the Hadith of Ghadir, establishing Imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shi'a interpret the word 'mawla' in the Hadith of Ghadir to mean 'the one in charge' and thus establish Imamate for Ali.

Core Idea
In the Hadith of Ghadir, 'mawla' means the one most entitled to act, which is equivalent to Imamate.

The previous lesson presented the Hadith of Ghadir. Now we delve into its interpretation. The key is the word 'mawla', which has multiple meanings. The Shi'a argue that in this context, it must mean 'the one in charge' or 'the one most entitled to act', a meaning that aligns with the concept of Imamate. This lesson examines the linguistic evidence and the reasoning behind this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
List the possible meanings of 'mawla'
The text lists seven possible meanings for 'mawla': freed slave, patron, ally, neighbor, cousin, helper, and the one more entitled to act. Read them in Arabic.
2
Understand the evidence for 'one entitled to act'
The text provides Quranic and prophetic examples where 'mawla' means 'more worthy' or 'in charge'. Examine these proofs.
The verse from the Quran: 'Your refuge is the Fire; it is your mawla' meaning more worthy.
3
Follow the elimination argument
The text argues that only the meaning 'one in charge' fits the context of the hadith, because the other meanings are obvious or irrelevant. Consider why.
The first five meanings are implausible, the sixth (helper) is too obvious to require a gathering.
4
Connect to Imamate
The text concludes that 'mawla' here means authority over people, which is exactly the meaning of Imamate. Understand this conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك بما يدعون فيه التواتر من الأخبار، أما حديث الغدير فهو أنه (عليه السلام) قد غدير خم موضع بين مكة والمدينة - بالجحفة وذلك بعد رجوعه من حجة الوداع. وكان يوماً صائفاً حتى إن الرجل ليضع رداءه تحت شدة الحر، وجمع الرحال، وصعد (عليه السلام) عليها وقال مخاطباً معاشر المسلمين : ألست أولى بكم من أنفسكم؟ قالوا: اللهم بلى. قال: فمن كنت مولاه فعلی ،مولاه اللهم وال من والاه وعاد من عاداه، وانصر من نصره خذله (۱) واخذل من وهذا حديث متفق على صحته أورده علي رضي الله عنه يوم الشورى عندما حاول ذكر فضائله، ولم ينكره أحد . ولفظ المولى قد يراد به المعتق، والمعتق، والحليف، والجار ، و ابن العم والناصر والأولى بالتصرف، قال الله تعالى: (مأواكم النار هي مولاكم ) (٢) أي أولى بكم ذكره أبو عبيدة وقال النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) : أيما امرأة نكحت نفسها بغير إذن مولاها (٣) . . أي الأولى بها والمالك لتدبير أمرها، ومثله في الشعر كثير. وبالجملة استعمال المولى بمعنى المتولي والمالك للأمر، والأولى بالتصرف شائع في كلام العرب منقول عن كثير من أئمة اللغة والمراد أنه اسم لهذا المعنى، لا صفة، بمنزلة الأولى ليعترض بأنه ليس من صيغة اسم التفضيل ، وأنه لما يستعمل استعماله، وينبغي أن يكون المراد به في الحديث هو هذا المعنى ليطابق صدر الحديث، ولأنه لا وجه للخمسة الأول وهو ظاهر ولا للسادس لظهوره وعدم ، احتياجه إلى البيان وجمع الناس لأجله، سيما وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿ والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ) (1) . ولا خفاء في أن الولاية بالناس والتولي والمالكية لتدبير أمرهم ، والتصرف فيهم بمنزلة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) هو معنى الإمامة .
They hold fast to what they claim to be mass-transmitted (tawatur) among the reports. As for the Hadith of Ghadir, it is that he (peace be upon him) was at Ghadir Khumm, a place between Mecca and Medina at al-Juhfa, after his return from the Farewell Pilgrimage. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'mawla' in the Hadith of Ghadir merely means 'friend' or 'helper' and does not imply leadership.
Correction: The text argues that 'mawla' in this context means 'one in charge' based on linguistic evidence and the elimination of other meanings, thereby establishing leadership (Imamate).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of 'mawla' is intended in the Hadith of Ghadir?
A) Helper (al-nasir)
B) Neighbor (al-jar)
C) The one more entitled to act (al-awla bi al-tasarruf)
D) Freed slave (al-mu'taq)

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a interpret 'mawla' in the Hadith of Ghadir as 'the one in charge' (al-mutawalli), which is synonymous with Imamate, thus making the hadith a clear designation of Ali as Imam.

?

?
The author argues that the gathering at Ghadir was for something important, not a mere reminder of friendship. How does your personal attachment to a particular school of thought affect your willingness to judge the evidence fairly?
?
If Ali was indeed designated as Imam at Ghadir, what does that imply about your current relationship with the Ahl al-Bayt? Are you willing to re-examine your position, or are you comfortable with the status quo?
