Denial of Tawatur in the Hadith of Ghadir
The author denies tawatur and cites scholarly criticism against the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument that the Hadith of Ghadir is not mutawatir and why this undermines the Shi'a proof.

Core Idea
A hadith must be mass-transmitted to serve as proof; the Ghadir report fails this test.

The Shi'a rely on the Hadith of Ghadir to claim Ali's immediate succession. Sunni scholars respond by first questioning the reliability of its transmission. This lesson examines the claim that the hadith is not mutawatir (mass-transmitted) and that many major hadith masters did not include it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on tawatur
Read the Arabic and translation of the opening refutation. Notice how the author immediately denies tawatur.
The denial is phrased as 'منع تواتر الخبر'.
2
Understand the criticism of the hadith
The author notes that many scholars impugned its authenticity and that Bukhari, Muslim, and al-Waqidi did not transmit it. Consider why this is significant.
These are the most authoritative hadith collectors.
3
Consider the missing preamble
The original argument for the Shi'a relies on a preamble that most narrators did not include. Without it, the word 'mawla' is ambiguous.
The preamble would specify 'the one more entitled'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع تواتر الخبر، فإن ذلك من مكابرات الشيعة، كيف وقد قدح في صحته كثير من أهل الحديث، ولم ينقله المحققون منهم كالبخاري ومسلم، والواقدي، وأكثر من رواه لم يرووا المقدمة التي جعلت دليلاً على أن المراد بالمولى الأولى، وبعد صحة الرواية فمؤخر الخبر - أعني قوله : اللهم وال من والاه ـ يشعر بأن المراد بالمولى هو الناصر والمحب، بل مجرد احتمال ذلك كاف في دفع الاستدلال، وما ذكره من أن ذلك معلوم ظاهر من قوله تعالى د والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ) (۲) لا يدفع الاحتمال لجواز أن يكون الغرض التنصيص على موالاته ونصرته ، ليكون أبعد عن التخصيص الذي تحتمله أكثر العمومات وليكون أقوى دلالة وأوفى بإفادة زيادة الشرف، حيث قرن بموالاة النبي ( وهذا القدر من المحبة والنصرة لا يقتضي ثبوت الإمامة، وبعد تسليم الدلالة على الإمامة فلا بمبرة بخبر الواحد في مقابلة الإجماع . ولوسلم، فغايته الدلالة على استحقاق الإمامة وثبوتها في المآل ، لكن من أين يلزم نفي إمامة الأئمة قبله، وهذا قول بالموجب ، وهو جواب ظاهر لم يذكره القوم ، وإذا تأملت فما يدعون من تواتر الخبر حجة عليهم، لا لهم، لأنه لو كان مسوقاً لثبوت الإمامة، دالاً عليه، لما خفي على عظماء الصحابة، فلم يتركوا الاستدلال ،به ولم يتوقفوا في أمر الإمامة . والقول بأن القوم تركوا الانقياد عناداً، وعلي (رضي الله عنه) ترك الاحتجاج تقية آية الغواية هو غاية الوقاحة .
The answer: denial of the mass transmission (tawatur) of the report, for that is among the fabrications of the Shi'a. How (can it be tawatur) when many hadith scholars have impugned its authenticity, and the reliable ones among them like al-Bukhari, Muslim, and al-Waqidi did not transmit it. And most of those who narrated it did not narrate the preamble which was made evidence that the intended meaning of 'mawla' is 'the one more entitled'. And even if the narration is authentic, the latter part of the hadith—i.e., his saying: 'O Allah, befriend him who befriends him'—indicates that the intended meaning of 'mawla' is the helper and the lover. Rather, the mere possibility of that is sufficient to refute the deduction. And what he mentioned, that it is known clearly from the verse 'And the believing men and believing women are allies (awliya) of one another', does not remove the possibility, because the purpose may be to specify his friendship and support, to be further from the specification that most general texts admit, and to be stronger in indication and more fulfilling in conveying additional honor, since it was paired with the friendship of the Prophet. And this amount of love and support does not necessitate the establishment of Imamate. And after granting the indication of Imamate, there is no benefit from a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) in opposition to consensus. And if it is granted, its utmost is the indication of deserving the Imamate and its establishment in the future; but where does it follow the negation of the Imamate of the imams before him? This is an affirmative statement (qawl bi al-mujab), and it is an obvious answer that the people did not mention. And if you reflect, what they claim of tawatur is an argument against them, not for them, because if it had been brought to establish Imamate and indicated it, it would not have been hidden from the great Companions; thus they would not have neglected to use it and would have not hesitated in the matter of Imamate. And the claim that the people refused out of obstinacy, and that Ali (ra) refrained from arguing out of taqiyya (dissimulation), is the height of insolence.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is the Sunni refutation of the Shi'a argument from the Hadith of Ghadir. The author first denies that the hadith is mutawatir (mass-transmitted), noting that many scholars have criticized its authenticity and that major muhaddithun like al-Bukhari, Muslim, and al-Waqidi did not include it. Moreover, most narrators did not include the introductory part that would establish the meaning of 'mawla' as 'the one more entitled'. Even if the hadith is authentic, the concluding supplication ('O Allah, befriend him who befriends him') suggests that 'mawla' means helper (nasir) and lover (muhibb), not ruler. The mere possibility of this alternative meaning is enough to undermine the Shi'a deduction. The author also argues that the verse about Muslims being allies to each other does not remove the possibility, because the hadith might simply be emphasizing love and support. Such love and support do not entail Imamate. Furthermore, even if the hadith indicated Imamate, it is a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) and cannot override consensus (ijma'). Moreover, the hadith at most indicates that Ali is deserving of Imamate in the future, but it does not negate the Imamate of the previous caliphs. The author points out that the Shi'a claim of tawatur is actually evidence against them, because if it were widely known and clearly indicated Imamate, the Companions would have used it and not disagreed about leadership. Finally, the author condemns as outrageous the claim that the Companions refused out of stubbornness and that Ali practiced taqiyya (dissimulation).

Key Terms
التواتر
خبر الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Ghadir is universally accepted as authentic and mutawatir by all Muslims.
Correction: Sunni scholars argue that it is not mutawatir and that many major hadith collectors rejected or omitted it.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is NOT a reason to deny tawatur of the Hadith of Ghadir?
A) Many scholars impugned its authenticity.
B) Al-Bukhari and Muslim did not narrate it.
C) The Shi'a fabricated the hadith entirely.
D) Most narrators omitted the preamble.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni response begins by challenging the mass transmission of the Ghadir hadith, thereby weakening its evidentiary value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious claim simply because it was widely repeated, without personally verifying its evidence? How does the author's call to examine the tawatur of a report challenge you to re-evaluate the foundations of your own beliefs? Will you commit to investigating the sources of your major theological convictions this week?
?
When you encounter a controversial hadith like that of Ghadir, do you immediately side with your own tradition or do you honestly weigh the scholarly criticisms? The author dismisses the Shi'a claim as 'mukabarah'. Is there a belief you hold that you have never seriously questioned because it is tied to your identity? What would it take for you to submit to the truth, even if it means revising a long-held position?
?
The author points out that even those who narrated the hadith omitted the crucial preamble. Are you selective in your own reading of texts, focusing only on what supports your view and ignoring context? How can you cultivate the discipline to read every proof in its full context, especially when it challenges your assumptions? What specific step will you take today to practice this in your study?

Alternative Meaning of 'Mawla' in the Ghadir Hadith
The author provides an alternative interpretation of 'mawla' as helper/lover and refutes the verse argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument that the word 'mawla' in the Hadith of Ghadir means helper and lover, not ruler.

Core Idea
The supplication indicates friendship, not political authority.

Even if the hadith is authentic, the author argues that its text points to a different meaning. The supplication 'O Allah, befriend him who befriends him' suggests that 'mawla' means helper (nasir) and lover (muhibb), not the one entitled to rule. This lesson explores that argument and the rebuttal to the Shi'a appeal to a Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the supplication
Read the part from 'وبعد صحة الرواية' until 'لا يقتضي ثبوت الإمامة'. Understand the argument.
Focus on the supplication phrase.
2
Analyze the meaning of 'mawla'
The author argues that the supplication shows 'mawla' means helper/lover. Consider why this is plausible.
Contrast with the Shi'a claim of 'first in authority'.
3
Examine the Quranic verse argument
The Shi'a use the verse about believers being awliya of each other. The author responds that the hadith may simply specify love and support for Ali.
The verse does not necessitate political leadership.
4
Conclude the argument
The author concludes that love and support do not prove Imamate. Note the logical step.
This is a key refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب منع تواتر الخبر، فإن ذلك من مكابرات الشيعة، كيف وقد قدح في صحته كثير من أهل الحديث، ولم ينقله المحققون منهم كالبخاري ومسلم، والواقدي، وأكثر من رواه لم يرووا المقدمة التي جعلت دليلاً على أن المراد بالمولى الأولى، وبعد صحة الرواية فمؤخر الخبر - أعني قوله : اللهم وال من والاه ـ يشعر بأن المراد بالمولى هو الناصر والمحب، بل مجرد احتمال ذلك كاف في دفع الاستدلال، وما ذكره من أن ذلك معلوم ظاهر من قوله تعالى د والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض ) (۲) لا يدفع الاحتمال لجواز أن يكون الغرض التنصيص على موالاته ونصرته ، ليكون أبعد عن التخصيص الذي تحتمله أكثر العمومات وليكون أقوى دلالة وأوفى بإفادة زيادة الشرف، حيث قرن بموالاة النبي ( وهذا القدر من المحبة والنصرة لا يقتضي ثبوت الإمامة، وبعد تسليم الدلالة على الإمامة فلا بمبرة بخبر الواحد في مقابلة الإجماع . ولوسلم، فغايته الدلالة على استحقاق الإمامة وثبوتها في المآل ، لكن من أين يلزم نفي إمامة الأئمة قبله، وهذا قول بالموجب ، وهو جواب ظاهر لم يذكره القوم ، وإذا تأملت فما يدعون من تواتر الخبر حجة عليهم، لا لهم، لأنه لو كان مسوقاً لثبوت الإمامة، دالاً عليه، لما خفي على عظماء الصحابة، فلم يتركوا الاستدلال ،به ولم يتوقفوا في أمر الإمامة . والقول بأن القوم تركوا الانقياد عناداً، وعلي (رضي الله عنه) ترك الاحتجاج تقية آية الغواية هو غاية الوقاحة .
The answer: denial of the mass transmission (tawatur) of the report, for that is among the fabrications of the Shi'a. How (can it be tawatur) when many hadith scholars have impugned its authenticity, and the reliable ones among them like al-Bukhari, Muslim, and al-Waqidi did not transmit it. And most of those who narrated it did not narrate the preamble which was made evidence that the intended meaning of 'mawla' is 'the one more entitled'. And even if the narration is authentic, the latter part of the hadith—i.e., his saying: 'O Allah, befriend him who befriends him'—indicates that the intended meaning of 'mawla' is the helper and the lover. Rather, the mere possibility of that is sufficient to refute the deduction. And what he mentioned, that it is known clearly from the verse 'And the believing men and believing women are allies (awliya) of one another', does not remove the possibility, because the purpose may be to specify his friendship and support, to be further from the specification that most general texts admit, and to be stronger in indication and more fulfilling in conveying additional honor, since it was paired with the friendship of the Prophet. And this amount of love and support does not necessitate the establishment of Imamate. And after granting the indication of Imamate, there is no benefit from a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) in opposition to consensus. And if it is granted, its utmost is the indication of deserving the Imamate and its establishment in the future; but where does it follow the negation of the Imamate of the imams before him? This is an affirmative statement (qawl bi al-mujab), and it is an obvious answer that the people did not mention. And if you reflect, what they claim of tawatur is an argument against them, not for them, because if it had been brought to establish Imamate and indicated it, it would not have been hidden from the great Companions; thus they would not have neglected to use it and would have not hesitated in the matter of Imamate. And the claim that the people refused out of obstinacy, and that Ali (ra) refrained from arguing out of taqiyya (dissimulation), is the height of insolence.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is the Sunni refutation of the Shi'a argument from the Hadith of Ghadir. The author first denies that the hadith is mutawatir (mass-transmitted), noting that many scholars have criticized its authenticity and that major muhaddithun like al-Bukhari, Muslim, and al-Waqidi did not include it. Moreover, most narrators did not include the introductory part that would establish the meaning of 'mawla' as 'the one more entitled'. Even if the hadith is authentic, the concluding supplication ('O Allah, befriend him who befriends him') suggests that 'mawla' means helper (nasir) and lover (muhibb), not ruler. The mere possibility of this alternative meaning is enough to undermine the Shi'a deduction. The author also argues that the verse about Muslims being allies to each other does not remove the possibility, because the hadith might simply be emphasizing love and support. Such love and support do not entail Imamate. Furthermore, even if the hadith indicated Imamate, it is a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) and cannot override consensus (ijma'). Moreover, the hadith at most indicates that Ali is deserving of Imamate in the future, but it does not negate the Imamate of the previous caliphs. The author points out that the Shi'a claim of tawatur is actually evidence against them, because if it were widely known and clearly indicated Imamate, the Companions would have used it and not disagreed about leadership. Finally, the author condemns as outrageous the claim that the Companions refused out of stubbornness and that Ali practiced taqiyya (dissimulation).

Key Terms
الناصر
المحب
المولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith can only mean 'master' or 'ruler'.
Correction: The author argues it can mean helper/lover, supported by the supplication in the hadith.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the phrase 'O Allah, befriend him who befriends him'?
A) It proves that Ali is the caliph.
B) It shows that 'mawla' means helper and lover.
C) It is a fabrication by the Shi'a.
D) It is not part of the original hadith.

Key Takeaway
Even if authentic, the Ghadir hadith's text supports an interpretation of love and support, not political authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Ghadir hadith, which interpretation naturally comes to your mind? Are you willing to consider that the text might support a different meaning, even if it challenges your prior assumptions? What would it cost you to accept that the hadith may not prove what you thought?
?
The author says that 'the mere possibility' of an alternative interpretation is enough to refute a deduction. In your own life, how often do you hold onto a belief based on a single interpretation of a text, event, or person's words, without honestly considering other possibilities? Can you think of one area where you need to apply this principle today?
?
If the Ghadir hadith only shows that the Prophet loved and supported Ali, but does not establish political authority, does that change how you view the early history of Islam? Are you willing to reexamine your loyalties and assumptions about the companions and the caliphate, even if it unsettles your heart?

Khabar al-Wahid, Ijma, and the Insults of Taqiyya
The author concludes with arguments from usul al-fiqh and a refutation of the taqiyya claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni arguments that even if the hadith indicated Imamate, it would be a solitary report overruled by consensus, and that the Shi'a claim of taqiyya is baseless.

Core Idea
A solitary report cannot override consensus; and the accusation of taqiyya is an insult to the Companions.

The author concludes his refutation with several powerful points: even granting that the hadith points to Imamate, it is a solitary report (khabar al-wahid) and cannot override the consensus of the Companions. Moreover, the hadith at most indicates future merit, not present succession. The Shi'a claim of tawatur actually backfires, and the accusation that the Companions acted out of obstinacy or that Ali practiced taqiyya is scandalous. This lesson unpacks these final arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the remainder of the passage
Read from 'وبعد تسليم الدلالة على الإمامة' to the end. Note the sequence of arguments.
The author uses a concessive structure: even if we accept X, then Y.
2
Understand the solitary report vs consensus
The author says a solitary report is of no benefit against consensus. Consider why consensus is stronger.
In Sunni usul, ijma is a primary source.
3
Analyze the 'affirmative statement' argument
The hadith at most shows Ali deserves imamate in the future, but does not negate previous imams. This is a 'qawl bi al-mujab' argument.
The author notes this is an obvious point the Shi'a missed.
4
Consider the tawatur claim backfiring
If the hadith were mutawatir and clearly about imamate, the Companions would have used it. Their not doing so is evidence against the Shi'a.
This is an argument from silence.
5
Refute the taqiyya accusation
The author condemns the claim that Ali practiced taqiyya as 'the height of insolence'. Reflect on the rhetoric.
Taqiyya is a Shi'a concept that Sunnis reject in this context.

Refuting the Shia Argument from the Hadith of Ghadir
The author refutes the Shia claim that the Ghadir hadith proves Ali's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multifaceted Sunni critique of the Shia use of the Ghadir hadith, including issues of authenticity, interpretation, and historical plausibility.

Core Idea
The Ghadir hadith does not prove imamate; it can mean friendship, and its transmission is not mass-transmitted.

The Hadith of Ghadir ('Man kuntu mawlahu') is a central proof for Shia claims of Ali's immediate succession. Sunni scholars respond by questioning the hadith's authenticity, its interpretation, and its legal weight. In this passage, the author systematically dismantles the Shia argument, offering multiple independent refutations that together undercut the claim. Let us follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shia claim
Read the passage and locate where the Shia argument is summarized: the hadith is mutawatir, clear, and 'mawla' means master, thus imamate.
The Shia claim is presented indirectly as the objection.
2
First refutation: Denial of tawatur
Notice the author's immediate response: the hadith is not mutawatir. He cites that major scholars like Bukhari and Muslim did not narrate it, and many questioned its authenticity.
This attacks the foundation of the proof.
3
Second refutation: Meaning of 'mawla'
Even if authentic, the phrase 'Allahumma wali man walah' indicates 'mawla' means friend/helper, not ruler. Mere possibility suffices to break the inference.
The Quranic verse 9:71 ('believers are allies') does not remove this ambiguity.
4
Third refutation: Legal weight and consensus
The hadith, even if interpreted as imamate, is a solitary report (khabar wahid) and cannot override consensus (ijma').
5
Fourth refutation: Temporal scope
The hadith at most proves Ali deserves imamate in the future, not that he should be the immediate successor. This neutralizes the argument against the first three caliphs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحديث، ولأنه لا وجه للخمسة الأول وهو ظاهر ولا للسادس لظهوره وعدم، احتياجه إلى البيان وجمع الناس لأجله، سيما وقد قال الله تعالى: {والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض}. ولا خفاء في أن الولاية بالناس والتولي والمالكية لتدبير أمرهم، والتصرف فيهم بمنزلة النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) هو معنى الإمامة. والجواب منع تواتر الخبر، فإن ذلك من مكابرات الشيعة، كيف وقد قدح في صحته كثير من أهل الحديث، ولم ينقله المحققون منهم كالبخاري ومسلم، والواقدي، وأكثر من رواه لم يرووا المقدمة التي جعلت دليلاً على أن المراد بالمولى الأولى، وبعد صحة الرواية فمؤخر الخبر - أعني قوله: اللهم وال من والاه - يشعر بأن المراد بالمولى هو الناصر والمحب، بل مجرد احتمال ذلك كاف في دفع الاستدلال، وما ذكره من أن ذلك معلوم ظاهر من قوله تعالى {والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض} لا يدفع الاحتمال لجواز أن يكون الغرض التنصيص على موالاته ونصرته، ليكون أبعد عن التخصيص الذي تحتمله أكثر العمومات وليكون أقوى دلالة وأوفى بإفادة زيادة الشرف، حيث قرن بموالاة النبي وهذا القدر من المحبة والنصرة لا يقتضي ثبوت الإمامة، وبعد تسليم الدلالة على الإمامة فلا بمبرة بخبر الواحد في مقابلة الإجماع. ولوسلم، فغايته الدلالة على استحقاق الإمامة وثبوتها في المآل، لكن من أين يلزم نفي إمامة الأئمة قبله، وهذا قول بالموجب، وهو جواب ظاهر لم يذكره القوم، وإذا تأملت فما يدعون من تواتر الخبر حجة عليهم، لا لهم، لأنه لو كان مسوقاً لثبوت الإمامة، دالاً عليه، لما خفي على عظماء الصحابة، فلم يتركوا الاستدلال به ولم يتوقفوا في أمر الإمامة. والقول بأن القوم تركوا الانقياد عناداً، وعلي (رضي الله عنه) ترك الاحتجاج تقية آية الغواية هو غاية الوقاحة.
The hadith, and because there is no aspect for the first five (reasons?) and it is apparent, nor for the sixth due to its clarity and lack of need for explanation and gathering people for its sake, especially since Allah Almighty said: {The believing men and believing women are allies of one another}. And it is not hidden that authority over the people, guardianship, and ownership to manage their affairs and act in them in the position of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is the meaning of imamate. The answer is to deny the tawatur (mass transmission) of the report, for that is among the exaggerations of the Shia. How so, when many hadith scholars have questioned its authenticity, and the verifiers among them such as al-Bukhari, Muslim, and al-Waqidi did not transmit it. And most of those who narrated it did not narrate the introduction that was made the proof that what is meant by 'mawla' is 'first with authority'. And after accepting the authenticity of the narration, the conclusion of the report - I mean his saying: 'O Allah, be a friend to whoever is his friend' - indicates that what is meant by 'mawla' is the helper and the beloved. Rather, mere possibility of that is sufficient to repel the inference. And what he mentioned that that is known and apparent from Allah's saying {The believing men and believing women are allies of one another} does not repel the possibility, for it is permissible that the purpose is to emphasize his friendship and support, so that it would be further from the specification that most general statements are subject to, and to be stronger in indication and more fulfilling in conveying increase of honor, since it was linked to the friendship of the Prophet. And this amount of love and support does not necessitate the establishment of imamate. And after conceding the indication of imamate, there is no value in a solitary report in the face of consensus. And if conceded, its utmost is the indication of deserving imamate and its establishment in the future. But from where is it necessary to negate the imamate of the imams before him? This is an admission of the point, and it is an apparent answer which the people did not mention. And if you reflect, what they claim of tawatur of the report is an argument against them, not for them, because if it were intended to establish imamate and indicating it, it would not have been hidden from the great companions, so they would not have abandoned using it as evidence and would not have hesitated in the matter of imamate. And the claim that the people left compliance out of obstinacy, and that Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) left argumentation out of taqiyya (dissimulation) is the height of shamelessness.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the Shia use of the Hadith of Ghadir (the 'man kuntu mawlahu' hadith) to prove Ali's immediate succession (imamate). The passage first presents the Shia objection: that the hadith is mutawatir (mass-transmitted) and clear, and that the term 'mawla' in it means 'master' or 'ruler', i.e., the one with authority over the believers like the Prophet, hence imamate. The author then provides a multi-layered refutation. First, he denies the tawatur of the report, noting that many hadith critics questioned its authenticity and that major verifiers like Bukhari, Muslim, and al-Waqidi did not narrate it, and even those who did often omitted the key phrase ('man kuntu mawlahu fa 'Aliyyun mawlahu') that makes the argument. Second, even if the report is authentic, the phrase 'Allahumma wali man walah' (O Allah, befriend whoever befriends him) suggests 'mawla' means 'friend' or 'supporter', not 'ruler'; mere probability of this meaning is enough to invalidate the proof. Third, the Shia's appeal to Quranic verse 9:71 (believers are allies of each other) does not remove the possibility of the friendship meaning. Fourth, even if the hadith indicated imamate, it is a solitary report (khabar wahid) and cannot override the consensus (ijma') of the community. Fifth, even accepting the indication, it only proves that Ali deserved imamate in the future (after the previous caliphs), not that he should have been the immediate successor. Finally, the author notes a powerful argument: if the hadith were truly clear and mutawatir for imamate, it would not have been hidden from the great Companions, and they would not have ignored it. The claim that they acted out of obstinacy and that Ali practiced taqiyya is outrageous. The author's approach is systematic: he attacks the transmission, the interpretation, the legal weight, and the historical evidence.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawatur)
خبر واحد (khabar wahid)
المولى (al-mawla)
الإمامة (al-imamah)
الإجماع (ijma')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that 'mawla' in the Ghadir hadith unequivocally means 'master' or 'ruler', and that the hadith is universally accepted as mutawatir.
Correction: The author shows that 'mawla' can mean 'friend' or 'supporter', and mere possibility of this meaning invalidates the proof. Additionally, the hadith's authenticity is disputed, and it is not considered mutawatir by leading muhaddithun.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the Shia claim that the Ghadir hadith is mutawatir?
A) It is a solitary report and many hadith critics questioned its authenticity.
B) The hadith is fabricate and unanimously rejected.
C) The hadith only applies to the Prophet's lifetime.
D) The hadith has no textual basis in any collection.

Key Takeaway
The Ghadir hadith, even if authentic, does not prove immediate imamate due to alternative interpretations, its status as a solitary report, and the historical fact that the Companions did not understand it as a designation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Shia claim the Companions acted out of obstinacy and Ali practiced taqiyya, does your heart recoil? Or do you find yourself sympathetic to the Shia narrative? Where does your loyalty truly lie—with the consensus of the Companions or with a sectarian claim that accuses them of treachery? Will you honestly examine your own biases and submit to the evidence, even if it challenges a narrative you were taught?
?
The author says that if the hadith were clearly about imamate, the great Companions would not have hesitated. Yet you and I often cling to a single hadith without considering the broader consensus. When you read a hadith that seems to support your preferred view, do you pause to ask: Did the Companions understand it that way? Are you willing to let their practice correct your interpretation?
?
The argument from the hadith of Ghadir has been used for centuries to divide the Ummah. After reading this refutation, can you honestly say you have no trace of partisanship in your heart? Will you pray to Allah to unite your heart with the truth, even if it means letting go of a cherished opinion?

6 min
Refuting the Shia Argument from the Hadith of Manzilah
The author refutes the Shia claim that the 'Manzilah' hadith proves Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Shia claim that the Hadith of Manzilah proves Ali's immediate succession.

Core Idea
The Hadith of Manzilah does not prove succession because Harun's authority came from prophethood.

In this lesson, we delve into a key sectarian debate concerning the succession of the Prophet. The Shia rely on the famous Hadith of Manzilah to argue for Ali's immediate caliphate. The author presents a detailed linguistic and theological refutation, showing that the hadith cannot carry the weight of such a claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shia argument
Read the first part of the passage where the Shia reasoning is outlined: 'manzilah' as a generic noun implying generality, with prophethood excepted, leaving all other positions including succession.
Notice the claim that the exception proves the rule of generality.
2
Examine the author's linguistic refutation
The author denies the hadith's tawatur, denies the generality of 'manzilah', and argues that the exception is disconnected (munqati'), not a true extraction.
Focus on the phrase 'استثناء منقطع' and its implications.
3
Grasp the theological refutation
Even if generality were conceded, Harun's authority (including succession) derived from his prophethood, which Ali lacked. The author also notes that Harun's deputyship was temporary and not a permanent succession after death.
Consider why the author compares Ali and Harun's prophetic status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حديث المنزلة فهو قوله (عليه السلام) لعلي (رضي الله عنه): «أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي» وتقريره أن المنزلة اسم جنس أضيف فعم، كما إذا عرف باللام بدليل صحة الاستثناء، وإذا استثنى منها مرتبة النبوة، بقيت عامة في باقي المنازل التي من جملتها كونه خليفة له ومتولياً تدبير الأمر، ومتصرفاً في مصالح العامة ورئيساً مفترض الطاعة لو عاش بعده. إذ لا يليق بمرتبة النبوة زوال هذه المنزلة الرفيعة الثابتة في حياة موسى (عليه السلام) بوفاته. وإذا قد صرح بنفي النبوة، لم يكن ذلك إلا بطريق الإمامة. والجواب منع التواتر بل هو خبر واحد في مقابلة الإجماع، ومنع عموم المنازل، بل غاية الاسم المفرد المضاف إلى العلم الإطلاق. وربما يدعى كونه معهوداً معيناً كغلام زيد. وليس الاستثناء المذكور إخراجاً لبعض أفراد المنزلة، بمنزلة قولك: إلا النبوة. بل منقطع بمعنى لكن على مالا يخفى على أهل العربية، فلا يدل على العموم. كيف ومن منازله الأخوة في النسب، ولم يثبت لعلي، اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنها بمنزلة المستثنى لظهور انتفائها، ولو سلم العموم، فليس من منازل هارون الخلافة والتصرف بطريق النيابة على ما هو مقتضى الإمامة، لأنه شريك له في النبوة، وقوله: اخلفني ليس استخلافاً بل مبالغة وتأكيداً في القيام بأمر القوم. ولو سلم فلا دلالة على بقائها بعد الموت وليس انتفاؤها بموت المستخلف عزلاً ولا نقصاً، بل ربما يكون عوداً إلى حالة أكمل، هي الاستقلال بالنبوة، والتبليغ من الله، ولوسلم، فتصرف هارون ونفاذ أمره لو بقي بعد موسى إنما يكون لنبوته. وقد انتفت النبوة في حق علي رضي الله تعالى عنه فينتفي ما يبتنى عليها ويتسبب عنها. وأما الجواب بأن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم) لما خرج إلى غزوة تبوك استخلف علياً على المدينة، فأكثر أهل النفاق في ذلك. فقال علي يا رسول الله، أتتركني مع الخوالف؟
As for the Hadith of Manzilah (Position), it is his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'You are to me in the position of Harun to Musa, except that there is no prophet after me.' And its articulation is that 'manzilah' (position) is a generic noun that when added (to a proper noun) becomes general, just as when it is defined by the definite article, as evidenced by the validity of the exception. And when the rank of prophethood is excepted from it, it remains general in the remaining positions, among which is being his successor (khalifah), managing affairs, acting for the public welfare, and being a leader whose obedience is obligatory if he had lived after him. For it is not befitting for the rank of prophethood that this lofty position established in the life of Musa (peace be upon him) should cease with his death. And when prophethood has been explicitly negated, that cannot be except by way of imamate. The answer is to deny tawatur; rather, it is a solitary report in the face of consensus, and to deny the generality of the positions. Rather, the utmost of a singular noun added to a proper noun is absolute reference. And perhaps it is claimed to be a specific known reference like 'the slave of Zaid'. And the mentioned exception is not an extraction of some individuals of the position, like your saying 'except prophethood'. Rather, it is disconnected (munqati') meaning 'but', as is not hidden from those versed in Arabic, so it does not indicate generality. How so, when among his positions is brotherhood in lineage, and that was not established for Ali, unless it is said: that it is like the excepted thing due to its obvious non-existence. And if generality is conceded, then succession (khilafah) and acting by way of deputyship as required by imamate is not among the positions of Harun, because he was a partner in prophethood, and his saying 'Khulfni' (Be my successor) is not a designation of succession but rather exaggeration and emphasis in undertaking the people's affair. And if it is conceded, there is no indication of its continuation after death, and its cessation by the death of the one designated as successor is not a dismissal nor a deficiency; rather, perhaps it is a return to a more complete state, which is independence in prophethood and conveying from Allah. And if it is conceded, the authority of Harun and the execution of his command if he had remained after Musa would only be due to his prophethood. And prophethood has been negated in the case of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), so what is built upon it and results from it is negated. As for the answer that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) went out to the expedition of Tabuk, he appointed Ali over Medina, and many of the hypocrites spoke about that, so Ali said: O Messenger of Allah, do you leave me with the women and children?
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument concerning the Shia use of the Hadith of Manzilah to prove Ali's imamate. The Shia argument is presented first: The Prophet said to Ali, 'You are to me as Harun was to Musa, except there is no prophet after me.' The Shia reason that since 'manzilah' is a generic noun in annexation (idafah), it implies generality, and the exception of prophethood leaves all other positions intact, including being a successor, ruler, and leader. Therefore, Ali must have been the immediate successor (imam). The author then gives a detailed refutation. First, he denies the tawatur of the hadith, calling it a solitary report (khabar wahid) and thus insufficient against consensus. Second, he denies that 'manzilah' when added to a proper noun implies generality; it may simply be absolute or even denote a specific known reference. The exception is not a proper exception (istithna' muttasil) but a disconnected one (munqati'), meaning 'but' (lakin), which does not indicate that the other positions are included. Third, he points out that some positions of Harun, like physical brotherhood, did not apply to Ali. Fourth, even if generality were conceded, Harun's role of succession and deputyship was due to his prophethood, not a separate position; his authority derived from being a prophet. Since Ali was not a prophet, he cannot inherit that authority. Fifth, even the deputyship (khilafah) of Harun during Musa's absence was not a permanent succession but a temporary emphasis; it does not imply continuity after death. Finally, the author mentions an additional Shia argument from the Tabuk expedition, but the text cuts off. The overall approach is to undermine the linguistic basis of the Shia inference and to sever the analogy between Harun's authority and Ali's.

Key Terms
منزلة (manzilah)
استثناء منقطع (istithna' munqati')
خبر واحد (khabar wahid)
إجماع (ijma')
الخلافة (al-khilafah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The exception in the Hadith of Manzilah implies that all other positions of Harun (including succession) apply to Ali.
Correction: The exception is disconnected (munqati'), not a true exclusion, so it does not indicate generality. Moreover, Harun's succession role was due to his prophethood, which Ali did not have.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that the Hadith of Manzilah shows Ali is the successor?
A) He argues that the hadith is fabricated and has no authentic chain.
B) He argues that 'manzilah' is not general and that Harun's authority was based on prophethood.
C) He argues that the hadith refers only to the Battle of Tabuk.
D) He argues that Ali himself rejected this interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The Hadith of Manzilah, when properly analyzed, does not support the Shia claim of immediate succession for Ali because the linguistic evidence is weak and Harun's authority was intrinsically tied to his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a beloved figure or a famous hadith is used to support a belief that contradicts the established understanding of the majority of scholars, do you examine the evidence critically, or does emotional attachment lead you to accept it uncritically? This lesson shows the importance of rigorous analysis—where do you need to apply that discipline in your own faith journey?
?
The author dismantles a seemingly straightforward proof by examining the language, transmission, and theological implications. Is there an area of your religion where you rely on a single proof or a simplistic interpretation without considering the broader framework? What is one step you can take today to dig deeper into that issue?
?
This lesson demonstrates that even a direct statement from the Prophet can be misunderstood if taken out of proper context. How do you ensure that you are not misinterpreting Islamic texts to fit your own desires or preconceived notions? The Shia Argument and Initial Refutation
Presentation of Shia argument and initial refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia argument from the hadith of position and the initial Sunni refutation based on transmission and linguistic analysis.

Core Idea
The hadith of position fails as proof for imamate due to weak transmission and linguistic ambiguity.

The Shia rely on the hadith of al-manzila to argue that Ali was designated as the Prophet's successor. In this lesson, we examine the claim's logical structure and the first part of its refutation, focusing on issues of transmission and the meaning of the exception.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia argument
Read the first part of the passage where the Shia present their interpretation of the hadith, noting the claim that 'position' (منزلة) is a generic noun that becomes general when added to a proper noun.
Pay attention to the reasoning that the exception of prophethood leaves all other positions, including caliphate.
2
Identify the refutation points
The author gives three initial refutations: denial of tawatur, denial of generality, and that the exception is disconnected. Find each in the text and understand their meaning.
3
Understand the linguistic argument
Focus on why the exception is considered 'disconnected' (منقطع) and how that affects the claim of generality. This is a key point in Arabic grammar.
The exception does not function as a true extraction from a general term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حديث المنزلة فهو قوله (عليه السلام) لعلي (رضي الله عنه) : «أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي» (۱) وتقريره أن المنزلة اسم جنس أضيف فعم ، كما إذا عرف باللام بدليل صحة الاستثناء، وإذا استثنى منها مرتبة النبوة، بقيت عامة في باقي المنازل التي من جملتها كونه خليفة له ومتولياً تدبير الأمر، ومتصرفاً في مصالح العامة ورئيساً مفترض الطاعة لو عاش بعده . إذ لا يليق بمرتبة النبوة زوال هذه المنزلة الرفيعة الثابتة في حياة موسى (عليه السلام) بوفاته . وإذا قد صرح بنفي النبوة ، لم يكن ذلك إلا بطريق الإمامة .
والجواب منع التواتر بل هو خبر واحد في مقابلة الإجماع، ومنع عموم المنازل، بل غاية الاسم المفرد المضاف إلى العلم الإطلاق. وربما يدعى كونه معهوداً معيناً كغلام زيد. وليس الاستثناء المذكور إخراجاً لبعض أفراد المنزلة، بمنزلة قولك : إلا النبوة. بل منقطع بمعنى لكن على مالا يخفى على أهل العربية، فلا يدل على العموم . كيف ومن منازله الأخوة في النسب ، ولم يثبت لعلي ، اللهم إلا أن : يقال : إنها بمنزلة المستثنى لظهور انتفائها، ولو سلم العموم، فليس من منازل هارون الخلافة والتصرف بطريق النيابة على ما هو مقتضى الإمامة، لأنه شريك له في النبوة، وقوله : اخلفني (۱) ليس استخلافاً بل مبالغة وتأكيداً في القيام بأمر القوم . ولو سلم فلا دلالة على بقائها بعد الموت وليس انتفاؤها بموت المستخلف عزلا ولا نقصاً ، بل ربما يكون عوداً إلى حالة أكمل ، هي الاستقلال بالنبوة، والتبليغ من الله، ولوسلم ، فتصرف، هارون ونفاذ أمره لو بقي بعد موسى إنما يكون لنبوته . وقد انتفت النبوة في حق علي رضي الله تعالى عنه فينتفي ما يبتنى عليها ويتسبب عنها.
وأما الجواب بأن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم ) لما خرج إلى غزوة تبوك استخلف علياً على المدينة، فأكثر أهل النفاق في ذلك . فقال علي يا رسول الله ، أتتركني مع الخوالف؟
فقال : أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى ، إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي . وهذا لا يدل على خلافته بعده ، كابن أم مكتوم (۱) ( رضي الله عنه) استخلفه على المدينة في كثير من غزواته، فربما يدفع بأن العبرة لعموم اللفظ، لا لخصوص السبب بل ربما يحتج بأن استخلافه على المدينة وعدم عزله منها، مع أنه لا قائل بالفصل ، وأن الاحتياج إلى الخليفة بعد الوفاة أشد وأوكد منه حال الغيبة يدل على كونه خليفة
As for the hadith of the position, it is his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'You are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me.' Its presentation [by the opponent] is that 'position' is a generic noun added [to a proper noun] and thus generalizes, like when it is defined with the definite article, as evidenced by the validity of the exception. When the rank of prophethood is excepted from it, the rest remains general over the remaining positions, among them being his being a caliph for him, taking charge of the administration of affairs, managing the public interests, and a leader whose obedience is obligatory had he lived after him. For it is not befitting the rank of prophethood that this lofty position, established during the life of Moses (peace be upon him), should disappear with his death. And since he explicitly negated prophethood, that could only be by way of imamate. The response: denial of tawātur (mass transmission); rather it is a solitary report (khabar wāḥid) in the face of consensus (ijmāʿ); and denial of the generality of the positions; rather the utmost of a singular noun added to a proper noun is absolute reference (iṭlāq). Perhaps it is claimed to be a specific known [instance] like 'the slave of Zayd'. And the mentioned exception is not an extraction of some individuals of the position, like your saying 'except prophethood'. Rather it is disconnected (munqaṭiʿ) in the sense of 'but' (lākin), as is not hidden from those proficient in Arabic, so it does not indicate generality. How [could it be general] while among his positions is brotherhood in lineage, which was not established for Ali. Except if it is said that it is like the excepted [item] due to the apparent negation of it. And if generality is granted, then deputyship (khilāfa) and acting by way of representation, as required by imamate, are not among the positions of Aaron, because he was a partner with him in prophethood, and his saying 'Take my place' is not a deputyship (istikhlāf) but rather an exaggeration and emphasis in undertaking the affairs of the people. And if it is granted, there is no indication of its continuation after death, and its being negated by the death of the one appointed is neither removal nor deficiency; rather perhaps it is a return to a more perfect state, which is independence in prophethood and conveying from Allah. And if it is granted, then the authority of Aaron and the execution of his command, had he remained after Moses, would only be due to his prophethood. And prophethood has been negated in the case of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), so that which is built upon it and results from it is negated. As for the response that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) went out to the expedition of Tabuk, he appointed Ali over Medina, and the hypocrites talked much about that, so Ali said, 'O Messenger of Allah, do you leave me with the women and children?' He said, 'Are you not satisfied that you are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' This does not indicate his caliphate after him, like Ibn Umm Maktum (may Allah be pleased with him) whom he appointed over Medina in many of his campaigns. Perhaps it is rebutted by saying that consideration is for the generality of the wording, not for the specific cause. But perhaps it is argued that his appointment over Medina and his not being removed from it, while there is no one who distinguishes [between the two cases], and that the need for a caliph after death is more severe and more emphatic than during absence, indicates that he is the caliph.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents a Shia argument based on the hadith of al-manzila (the hadith of position) and refutes it point by point. The Shia claim is that when the Prophet said to Ali 'You are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me,' the word 'position' is a generic noun added to a proper noun, making it general. Since the exception excludes only prophethood, all other positions of Aaron — including caliphate, leadership, and obedience — are affirmed for Ali, and since he is not a prophet, these are realized through imamate (successor). The author refutes this by denying that the hadith is mutawatir (massively transmitted); it is a solitary report in the face of consensus. He also denies the claimed generality: a singular noun added to a proper noun merely indicates absolute reference, not generality, and the exception is 'disconnected' (munqatiʿ), meaning it is not a true exception from a general term. Moreover, some of Aaron's positions (like blood brotherhood) did not apply to Ali. Even if generality were granted, Aaron's deputyship (when Moses said 'take my place') was not a true istikhlaf in the sense of succession after death, but a temporary charge during absence, and his authority derived from his prophethood. Since prophethood is negated for Ali, any authority built on it is also negated. Finally, the author addresses a further objection: the incident of Tabuk where the Prophet appointed Ali over Medina and gave the same hadith. He argues that this was a temporary appointment like that of Ibn Umm Maktum, not proof of succession after death. The counter-argument that 'consideration is for the generality of the wording' and that the need is greater after death is mentioned but presented without endorsement, suggesting it is weak.

Key Terms
حديث المنزلة
منزلة
تواتر
خبر واحد
منقطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of al-manzila directly designates Ali as the Prophet's successor.
Correction: The hadith is not mutawatir and its wording does not indicate generality; the exception is disconnected and does not affirm all positions of Aaron.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one reason the hadith of al-manzila does not prove Ali's caliphate?
A) It is mutawatir and thus reliable.
B) The exception is disconnected, not indicating generality.
C) It was abrogated by a later verse.
D) The hadith explicitly mentions caliphate.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of position fails as proof for imamate due to its weak transmission and linguistic analysis that denies generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a religious argument that sounds compelling, do you first check its transmission and linguistic accuracy, or do you accept it because it feels right? What will you change after this lesson?
?
This lesson shows how a single hadith can be misinterpreted to support a major doctrine. Have you ever held a belief based on a single proof text without examining its context or counterarguments? Are you willing to revisit that belief now?
?
The refutation relies on precise Arabic grammar. If you rely on translations, you may miss such subtleties. How committed are you to learning the language of the Quran and hadith to avoid being misled? What step will you take this week?

Further Refutations: Aaron's Deputyship and Tabuk Incident
Continued refutation including Aaron's positions and Tabuk incident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper refutations of the hadith of position, including that Aaron's authority derived from his prophethood and that the Tabuk incident was a temporary appointment.

Core Idea
Aaron's authority came from his prophethood, which Ali did not have; the Tabuk incident was a temporary charge, not a designation for succession.

The previous lesson dealt with initial refutations. Here, the author continues by addressing further claims: that some of Aaron's positions like brotherhood do not apply to Ali, that his deputyship was not a true succession, and the famous incident at Tabuk is irrelevant for establishing caliphate after death.

Guided Path
1
Examine the points about Aaron's positions
Read the passage from 'كيف ومن منازله الأخوة' to 'فينتفي ما يبتنى عليها'. Note the arguments that brotherhood is not established, and that even if generality is granted, deputyship is not among Aaron's positions because he was a partner in prophethood.
The author also notes that 'اخلفني' is not a true istikhlaf but exaggeration.
2
Understand the argument from prophethood
The author argues that Aaron's authority (if he remained after Moses) would stem from his prophethood. Since Ali is not a prophet, any authority built on prophethood is negated.
3
Analyze the Tabuk incident
Read the part about the Prophet appointing Ali over Medina during Tabuk. The author argues this was temporary, like the appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum, and does not prove succession after death.
The counterargument that 'consideration is for the generality of the wording' is mentioned but not endorsed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حديث المنزلة فهو قوله (عليه السلام) لعلي (رضي الله عنه) : «أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي» (۱) وتقريره أن المنزلة اسم جنس أضيف فعم ، كما إذا عرف باللام بدليل صحة الاستثناء، وإذا استثنى منها مرتبة النبوة، بقيت عامة في باقي المنازل التي من جملتها كونه خليفة له ومتولياً تدبير الأمر، ومتصرفاً في مصالح العامة ورئيساً مفترض الطاعة لو عاش بعده . إذ لا يليق بمرتبة النبوة زوال هذه المنزلة الرفيعة الثابتة في حياة موسى (عليه السلام) بوفاته . وإذا قد صرح بنفي النبوة ، لم يكن ذلك إلا بطريق الإمامة .
والجواب منع التواتر بل هو خبر واحد في مقابلة الإجماع، ومنع عموم المنازل، بل غاية الاسم المفرد المضاف إلى العلم الإطلاق. وربما يدعى كونه معهوداً معيناً كغلام زيد. وليس الاستثناء المذكور إخراجاً لبعض أفراد المنزلة، بمنزلة قولك : إلا النبوة. بل منقطع بمعنى لكن على مالا يخفى على أهل العربية، فلا يدل على العموم . كيف ومن منازله الأخوة في النسب ، ولم يثبت لعلي ، اللهم إلا أن : يقال : إنها بمنزلة المستثنى لظهور انتفائها، ولو سلم العموم، فليس من منازل هارون الخلافة والتصرف بطريق النيابة على ما هو مقتضى الإمامة، لأنه شريك له في النبوة، وقوله : اخلفني (۱) ليس استخلافاً بل مبالغة وتأكيداً في القيام بأمر القوم . ولو سلم فلا دلالة على بقائها بعد الموت وليس انتفاؤها بموت المستخلف عزلا ولا نقصاً ، بل ربما يكون عوداً إلى حالة أكمل ، هي الاستقلال بالنبوة، والتبليغ من الله، ولوسلم ، فتصرف، هارون ونفاذ أمره لو بقي بعد موسى إنما يكون لنبوته . وقد انتفت النبوة في حق علي رضي الله تعالى عنه فينتفي ما يبتنى عليها ويتسبب عنها.
وأما الجواب بأن النبي (صلى الله عليه وسلم ) لما خرج إلى غزوة تبوك استخلف علياً على المدينة، فأكثر أهل النفاق في ذلك . فقال علي يا رسول الله ، أتتركني مع الخوالف؟
فقال : أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى ، إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي . وهذا لا يدل على خلافته بعده ، كابن أم مكتوم (۱) ( رضي الله عنه) استخلفه على المدينة في كثير من غزواته، فربما يدفع بأن العبرة لعموم اللفظ، لا لخصوص السبب بل ربما يحتج بأن استخلافه على المدينة وعدم عزله منها، مع أنه لا قائل بالفصل ، وأن الاحتياج إلى الخليفة بعد الوفاة أشد وأوكد منه حال الغيبة يدل على كونه خليفة
As for the hadith of the position, it is his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'You are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me.' Its presentation [by the opponent] is that 'position' is a generic noun added [to a proper noun] and thus generalizes, like when it is defined with the definite article, as evidenced by the validity of the exception. When the rank of prophethood is excepted from it, the rest remains general over the remaining positions, among them being his being a caliph for him, taking charge of the administration of affairs, managing the public interests, and a leader whose obedience is obligatory had he lived after him. For it is not befitting the rank of prophethood that this lofty position, established during the life of Moses (peace be upon him), should disappear with his death. And since he explicitly negated prophethood, that could only be by way of imamate. The response: denial of tawātur (mass transmission); rather it is a solitary report (khabar wāḥid) in the face of consensus (ijmāʿ); and denial of the generality of the positions; rather the utmost of a singular noun added to a proper noun is absolute reference (iṭlāq). Perhaps it is claimed to be a specific known [instance] like 'the slave of Zayd'. And the mentioned exception is not an extraction of some individuals of the position, like your saying 'except prophethood'. Rather it is disconnected (munqaṭiʿ) in the sense of 'but' (lākin), as is not hidden from those proficient in Arabic, so it does not indicate generality. How [could it be general] while among his positions is brotherhood in lineage, which was not established for Ali. Except if it is said that it is like the excepted [item] due to the apparent negation of it. And if generality is granted, then deputyship (khilāfa) and acting by way of representation, as required by imamate, are not among the positions of Aaron, because he was a partner with him in prophethood, and his saying 'Take my place' is not a deputyship (istikhlāf) but rather an exaggeration and emphasis in undertaking the affairs of the people. And if it is granted, there is no indication of its continuation after death, and its being negated by the death of the one appointed is neither removal nor deficiency; rather perhaps it is a return to a more perfect state, which is independence in prophethood and conveying from Allah. And if it is granted, then the authority of Aaron and the execution of his command, had he remained after Moses, would only be due to his prophethood. And prophethood has been negated in the case of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), so that which is built upon it and results from it is negated. As for the response that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) went out to the expedition of Tabuk, he appointed Ali over Medina, and the hypocrites talked much about that, so Ali said, 'O Messenger of Allah, do you leave me with the women and children?' He said, 'Are you not satisfied that you are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me?' This does not indicate his caliphate after him, like Ibn Umm Maktum (may Allah be pleased with him) whom he appointed over Medina in many of his campaigns. Perhaps it is rebutted by saying that consideration is for the generality of the wording, not for the specific cause. But perhaps it is argued that his appointment over Medina and his not being removed from it, while there is no one who distinguishes [between the two cases], and that the need for a caliph after death is more severe and more emphatic than during absence, indicates that he is the caliph.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage presents a Shia argument based on the hadith of al-manzila (the hadith of position) and refutes it point by point. The Shia claim is that when the Prophet said to Ali 'You are to me in the position of Aaron to Moses, except that there is no prophet after me,' the word 'position' is a generic noun added to a proper noun, making it general. Since the exception excludes only prophethood, all other positions of Aaron — including caliphate, leadership, and obedience — are affirmed for Ali, and since he is not a prophet, these are realized through imamate (successor). The author refutes this by denying that the hadith is mutawatir (massively transmitted); it is a solitary report in the face of consensus. He also denies the claimed generality: a singular noun added to a proper noun merely indicates absolute reference, not generality, and the exception is 'disconnected' (munqatiʿ), meaning it is not a true exception from a general term. Moreover, some of Aaron's positions (like blood brotherhood) did not apply to Ali. Even if generality were granted, Aaron's deputyship (when Moses said 'take my place') was not a true istikhlaf in the sense of succession after death, but a temporary charge during absence, and his authority derived from his prophethood. Since prophethood is negated for Ali, any authority built on it is also negated. Finally, the author addresses a further objection: the incident of Tabuk where the Prophet appointed Ali over Medina and gave the same hadith. He argues that this was a temporary appointment like that of Ibn Umm Maktum, not proof of succession after death. The counter-argument that 'consideration is for the generality of the wording' and that the need is greater after death is mentioned but presented without endorsement, suggesting it is weak.

Key Terms
منزلة
استخلاف
النبوة
الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Tabuk incident proves that Ali was to be the Prophet's successor after his death.
Correction: The Tabuk incident was a temporary appointment during the Prophet's absence, similar to other Companions who were appointed over Medina temporarily.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that Aaron's deputyship does not apply to Ali?
A) Aaron was not a deputy.
B) Aaron's authority came from his prophethood, which Ali did not have.
C) The hadith is about brotherhood.
D) The Tabuk incident shows it was temporary.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of position does not establish Ali's caliphate because the positions of Aaron were tied to his prophethood, and the Tabuk incident was merely a temporary appointment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a proof that challenges a belief you hold dear (like the Hadith of Position), what is your immediate reaction? Do you dismiss it, or do you pause to examine it honestly, even if it means uprooting something comfortable? Are you willing to follow the truth wherever it leads, regardless of prior loyalties?
?
This lesson shows that even seemingly obvious proofs can be dismantled by careful reasoning. How much time and effort do you invest in truly understanding the foundations of your faith—not just memorizing arguments, but critically weighing each link? Will you commit today to studying the sources with the same rigor as this author, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The author uses conditional statements: 'if granted, then...' repeatedly. When you hold a belief, do you ever consider the 'ifs'—the conditions that must be met for it to be true? Or do you assume it is simply true and avoid questioning? Ask yourself: are you intellectually humble enough to accept that some of your beliefs may rest on unproven conditions, and are you ready to seek the truth even if it costs you?

Explicit Texts Refuted: Ali's Succession Claims
Author lists and rebuts explicit texts for Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alleged explicit texts for Ali's succession and the author's refutation based on consensus and the isolated nature of these reports.

Core Idea
Explicit texts become weak when isolated against consensus.

In this lesson, the author addresses the claim that there are explicit prophetic texts designating Ali as the caliph. He lists five such texts and then provides a unified rebuttal, emphasizing that these are isolated reports (āḥād) that cannot override the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions. This passage illustrates a key method in Sunni polemics: using the principle of mass transmission and consensus to refute sectarian arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of explicit texts
Carefully read the five texts cited as alleged proofs for Ali's succession. Notice the Arabic phrasing and the brief explanations given.
Each text is introduced with 'such as' (مثل) indicating they are examples.
2
Understand each claim
For each text, consider what it explicitly states about Ali's position. The author includes a gloss for the first and last texts.
3
Study the rebuttal
The author gives two main points: these are isolated reports (āḥād) in the face of consensus, and if they were authentic and clear, they would not have been hidden from the Companions and the Prophet's family.
The rebuttal is concise but foundational.
4
Connect to methodology
Reflect on why the author uses consensus (ijmāʿ) and transmission quality (tawātur) as criteria. This is a standard Sunni approach to evaluate historical claims.
This lesson ties into broader principles of hadith criticism and communal authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الرابع - الرابع) - النصوص الجلية مثل : سلموا عليه بإمرة المؤمنين، أنت الخليفة من بعدي إنه إمام المتقين هذا خليفتي عليكم أنت أخي ووصي وخليفتي من بعدي وقاضي ديني (بكسر الدال) .
والجواب أنها آحاد في مقابلة الإجماع ولو صحت ودلت، لما خفيت على الصحابة ومن بعدهم ، سيما العترة الطاهرة .
هذه أخبار يدعون أنها نصوص جلية من النبي ( ) على خلافة علي (رضي الله تعالى عنه وهو قوله () مخاطباً لأصحابه : سلموا عليه بإمرة المؤمنين . الضمير لعلي والإمرة (بالكسر) الإمارة من أمر الرجل، صار أميراً. وقوله (عليه السلام) لعلي رضي الله تعالى عنه : أنت الخليفة من بعدي. وقوله (عليه السلام) : إنه إمام المتقين وقائد الغر المحجلين وقوله ( وقد أخذ بيد علي : هذا خليفتي عليكم . وقوله (عليه السلام) لعلي (رضي الله عنه : أنت أخي ووصبي وخليفتي من بعدي وقاضي ديني (بالكسر) . والجواب ما مر أنها أخبار آحاد في مقابلة الإجماع. وأنها لو صحت لما خفيت
He said: The fourth [proof] — the fourth — the explicit texts such as: 'Greet him with the title Commander of the Faithful,' 'You are the caliph after me,' 'He is the Imam of the pious,' 'This is my caliph over you,' 'You are my brother, my executor, and my caliph after me, and the liquidator of my debt' (with kasra on the dal). The response is that they are isolated reports (āḥād) in the face of consensus (ijmāʿ). Even if they were authentic and indicated [that], they would not have been hidden from the Companions and those after them, especially the pure progeny. These are reports they claim are explicit texts from the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the caliphate of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). And they are: his saying (peace be upon him), addressing his companions: 'Greet him with the title Commander of the Faithful' — the pronoun refers to Ali, and 'imra' (with kasra) means emirate, from the man becoming an emir; his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'You are the caliph after me'; his saying (peace be upon him): 'He is the Imam of the pious and the leader of the bright-faced ones'; his saying (peace be upon him), having taken Ali's hand: 'This is my caliph over you'; and his saying (peace be upon him) to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'You are my brother, my executor, and my caliph after me, and the liquidator of my debt' (with kasra). The response is what has passed: that they are isolated reports in the face of consensus, and that even if they were authentic, they would not have been hidden.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage presents a second set of alleged proofs for the caliphate of Ali: explicit texts (nusus jaliyya) from the Prophet. The author lists five such texts: (1) 'Greet him with the title Commander of the Faithful', (2) 'You are the caliph after me', (3) 'He is the Imam of the pious', (4) 'This is my caliph over you', and (5) 'You are my brother, my executor, and my caliph after me, and the liquidator of my debt'. Each is briefly explained. The author then gives a unified rebuttal: all these reports are solitary (āḥād), and they stand against the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions. Moreover, if they truly were authentic and clear in meaning, they would not have been hidden from the Companions and later generations, including the Prophet's own family (ʿitra ṭāhira). The argument is that the widespread acceptance of Abu Bakr's caliphate, including by Ali himself after initial reluctance, demonstrates these narrations are not as strong as claimed. The author concludes by repeating the two main points: they are isolated reports opposing consensus, and if authentic, they would have been well-known.

Key Terms

إمرة المؤمنين
الخليفة
وصي
إجماع
آحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'explicit texts' implies these narrations are undeniable proofs for Ali's succession.
Correction: The author argues that these texts are actually isolated reports (āḥād) which cannot override the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions, and if they were truly explicit, they would have been widely known.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main objection to the explicit texts (nusus jaliyya) cited for Ali's succession?
A) They are weak in chain of transmission.
B) They are isolated reports (āḥād) in the face of consensus (ijmāʿ).
C) They contradict the Quran.
D) They were invented later.

Key Takeaway
The alleged explicit texts for Ali's succession are not mass transmitted and are overruled by the consensus of the Companions, demonstrating a key Sunni methodological principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow the truth, but when evidence comes to you from a solitary source that challenges the consensus of the righteous, do you trust your isolated finding or submit to the collective? This lesson shows that even explicit texts, if solitary, cannot overturn consensus. In your own life, have you ever held onto a solitary opinion despite the clear agreement of those you trust? What will you do now to align with the community of believers?
?
The author argues that if these texts were truly authentic and clear, they would not have been hidden from the Companions and the pure progeny. Reflect on your own knowledge: are there matters of religion you think you know clearly, yet the scholars and the righteous have always held otherwise? How can you ensure that you are not being misled by a solitary report?
?
The rebuttal here is not just academic—it protects the unity of the Muslim community. Have you ever caused division by insisting on a point you thought was clear, only to find the Ummah had a different consensus? What does this lesson teach you about the importance of checking the strength and consensus behind your beliefs before acting on them?

Refuting Exclusive Succession from the Hadith of Manzila
Refutation of Shi'a argument from Hadith of Manzila using Ibn Umm Maktum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hadith of Manzila is refuted by citing counterexamples like Ibn Umm Maktum, and the implications for the debate on caliphate.

Core Idea
The Hadith of Manzila does not prove exclusive succession because similar appointments were made for others.

In this passage, the author addresses a key Shi'a proof for Ali's exclusive caliphate—the Prophet's statement comparing Ali to Harun. He counters by pointing out that the Prophet appointed others, like Ibn Umm Maktum, over Medina, showing that such a comparison does not entail exclusive succession. The passage also engages with potential objections based on general wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get a clear sense of the argument.
Notice the concise style of the refutation.
2
Identify the main argument and counterexample
The Shi'a argument uses the Hadith of Manzila. The author provides a counterexample: Ibn Umm Maktum was also appointed over Medina. Ask yourself why this undermines the exclusivity claim.
The key is that the Prophet made similar appointments for others.
3
Consider the rebuttal about general wording
The passage mentions a possible counter-rebuttal: consider the general wording rather than the specific cause. Think about how the author responds by reinforcing the point about need after death.
The discussion shows the depth of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال : أما ترضى أن تكون مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى ، إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي . وهذا لا يدل على خلافته بعده ، كابن أم مكتوم استخلفه على المدينة في كثير من غزواته، فربما يدفع بأن العبرة لعموم اللفظ، لا لخصوص السبب بل ربما يحتج بأن استخلافه على المدينة وعدم عزله منها، مع أنه لا قائل بالفصل ، وأن الاحتياج إلى الخليفة بعد الوفاة أشد وأوكد منه حال الغيبة يدل على كونه خليفة
He said: 'Are you not content to be from me in the position of Harun from Musa, except that there is no prophet after me?' This does not indicate his caliphate after him, like Ibn Umm Maktum – he appointed him over Medina in many of his expeditions. Perhaps it might be rebutted that consideration is for the generality of the wording, not for the specific cause. Rather, perhaps one might argue that his appointment over Medina and not removing him from it, along with the fact that no one makes a distinction, and that the need for a caliph after death is more severe and more confirmed than in the case of absence, indicates that he is a caliph.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Shi'a argument from the Hadith of Manzila, where the Prophet said to Ali, 'You are to me as Harun to Musa, except there is no prophet after me.' The author argues that this does not prove Ali's exclusive caliphate after the Prophet, because the Prophet also appointed others like Ibn Umm Maktum over Medina during his expeditions. The passage then considers a possible counter-argument: that the general wording of the hadith should be considered, not the specific circumstance. However, the author concludes by noting that the appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum and the fact that he was not removed, along with the greater need for a caliph after death, actually supports the idea of a caliph (but not necessarily Ali exclusively).

Key Terms
حديث المنزلة
ابن أم مكتوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hadith of Manzila proves Ali's exclusive right to the caliphate.
Correction: The author shows that the Prophet appointed others similarly, so the hadith does not imply exclusive succession.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does the author provide to challenge the claim that the Hadith of Manzila proves Ali's exclusive succession?
A) The appointment of Harun over Musa's people
B) The appointment of Ibn Umm Maktum over Medina
C) The appointment of Abu Bakr over the pilgrimage
D) The appointment of Umar over the judiciary

Key Takeaway
The Hadith of Manzila does not prove exclusive caliphate because the Prophet's similar appointments of others show the comparison does not entail exclusive succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith or verse that seems to support your theological position, do you pause to consider that others of equal faith might interpret it differently? What steps can you take to ensure you are not reading your own preferences into the text?
?
This lesson shows that the same kind of honorific comparison (like Harun) was given to another Companion. Are there any religious figures or leaders in your life you may have elevated to an exclusive level, forgetting that God's guidance is not monopolized by any single person?
?
The author uses logical reasoning to challenge a textual proof. Are you willing to subject your most cherished beliefs to the same kind of honest scrutiny, even if it means revising them? What would that require from you today?

Explicit Texts for Ali's Caliphate and Their Refutation
Author presents and refutes Shi'a claim of explicit texts for Ali's caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a claim of explicit texts (nusus jaliyya) for Ali's caliphate and the Sunni refutation based on transmission status, consensus, and the scope of what these texts prove.

Core Idea
Explicit-sounding texts cannot override established consensus and broader transmission.

This lesson presents a key sectarian argument: the Shi'a cite several explicit statements from the Prophet appointing Ali as his successor. The Sunni response challenges the reliability of these reports, their transmission status, and what they actually prove. Understanding this helps clarify the Sunni methodology of weighing evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the explicit texts claimed
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the various phrases the Shi'a consider explicit appointments of Ali as caliph.
These include 'Commander of the Believers', 'caliph after me', 'Imam of the righteous', etc.
2
Identify the Sunni response points
The author gives a three-part response. Find each part: (a) these are solitary reports (ahad) against consensus, (b) if authentic, they would not be hidden, (c) even if accepted, they only prove Ali's caliphate, not exclusivity.
The response is in 'والجواب أنها آحاد...' and continues.
3
Reflect on the role of consensus
Consider why the author emphasizes that these reports are 'ahad' in the face of 'ijma'. How does this reflect Sunni epistemology?
Consensus of the Companions carries more weight than isolated reports.
4
Analyze the 'even if accepted' argument
Note the third point: even if the texts are sound, they only establish Ali's caliphate, not that others were illegitimate. Why is this significant?
It allows for the possibility that Ali was worthy but not exclusively so.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the primary Sunni criticism.
The question focuses on the core objection regarding transmission.

Source Text
